Kearney. 6o The following resolutions were passed at the Conference :— (a) That it is advisable that some system of classification should be found possible in those cases where there are many vacancies on the normal roll of a school and yet children are refused admission owing to the fact that the vacancies do not exist in those particular classes to which they would naturally be allocated. (b) That this Conference is of opinion that Head-teachers in provided schools should, in all ordinary circumstances, be selected by the Local Managers of the School (where the vacancy exists) from three candidates on the promotion list prepared by the Education Committee of the London County Council. (c) That in the opinion of this Conference power should be given to Managers of schools of every kind to grant holidays, within limits that may be prescribed, on occasion of local festivities, etc. (d) That in the opinion of this Conference it would tend to greater efficiency in provided schools, and better feeling among the teachers, if all complaints of irregularities of conduct on the part of teachers were reported to the Managers of the schools in which they occur, instead of tothe Staffing Sub-Committee ; it then being the duty of the Managers to consider such complaints, to determine them, and finally to report to the Staffing Sub-Committee. (e) That the Borough Council and the Managers co-operate to carry out the new bye-laws re the employment of children of school age. (/) That the School Correspondents should supply at the monthly meetings figures to show the attendances of the Managers of the different groups of schools. 96. A copy of the resolutions was forwarded to the London County Council, to each of the Metropolitan Borough Councils, and to the Managers of the schools in the Borough. 97. The subject of the carrying out of small repairs at schools has been considered by the Council, and a resolution was passed to the effect that the Council recognised the urgent need of some means of carrying out these repairs more expeditiously than they are carried out under the existing system, and was of opinion that the Chairman of each body of School Managers should be empowered to order petty repairs of an urgent nature to be carried out at once, locally, submitting bills for the same periodically to the London County Council. Sites for Schools. 98. A site for a new school at the corner of Wimbledon Park Road and Standen Road, Wandsworth, was scheduled co the Education Board Provisional Order Confirmation (London, No. 1) Bill. The Council had 61 already approved of the site. The Bill received the Royal assent on the 4th August. 99. The County Council informed this Council that they had decided to take steps for the compulsory purchase of a site for a new school at Cambourn Road, Wandsworth. The Council approved the proposal, but called the attention of the County Council to the fact that this Council was not consulted before the site was determined upon as provided by Section 2 (2) of the Education (London) Act, 1903. 100. The County Council has also decided to increase the accommodation of the schools at Fountain Road, Franciscan Road, Broadwater Road and Smallwood Road. Southfields School site 101. With reference to the proposal of the London County Council to erect another school on part of the site of the Southfield Council School, referred to in the last annual report, a deputation from this Council waited upon a Sub-Committee of the Education Committee of the London County Council in support of this Council's objection to the proposal. The Council was subsequently informed that the SubCommittee had given careful consideration to the representations of the deputation, and that, having regard to the fact that the Board of Education had sanctioned the erection of a school on the site, the Sub-Committee were not prepared to recommend the Council to depart from its decision to erect the school. Higher Elementary Schools. 102. The subject of the higher elementary school accommodation in the Borough was considered by the Council, and the attention of the County Council was called to the necessity for the provision of further schools of this description, there being only one of these schools (Aristotle Road) in the Borough, and the Council suggested that the West Hill School, Wandsworth, should be converted into a higher elementary school, and other provision made for the ordinary scholars at the school. 103. The Aristotle Road School, Clapham, having been recognised as a higher elementary school, the Board of Education decided, under the provisions of Section 2 (3) of the Education 62 Act, 1903, that the school was not of a local character, and the school was thereupon withdrawn from the group to which it was attached, and a separate body of Managers appointed therefor. The County Council has appointed, as Managers of the school, the persons who were Managers of the group to which the school was formerly attached—Bonneville Road and Haselrigge Road. The Council is in communication with the London County Council as to the number of Managers of the two other schools, which, by reason of the withdrawal of the Aristotle Road, should be reduced from 18 to 12. Secondary Schools and Training College Accommodation. 104. The Council has had before it reports of the London County Council on the subject of the provision of secondary schools and training colleges, and asked the County Council to supply information as to proposed sites in this Borough. The County Council, however, stated that the negotiations were proceeding and that it was not desirable to make public the information, and pointed out that the County Council is empowered to take such steps as may seem desirable, after consultation with the Board of Education, to supply education other than elementary, and that this procedure differs entirely from that with regard to elementary education which requires the County Council to consult the Borough Council whenever it is proposed to acquire a site for a new public elementary school. The Council also learned from a report of the London County Council that such Council had purchased the house "Mayfield," at West Hill, Wandsworth, for a secondary school. This Council informed the London County Council that it viewed with great concern the acquisition of this house for a school on account of the consequent depreciation of the rateable value of property in the neighbourhood, and was of opinion that the site was very unsuitable, and also asked the County Council to invite the suggestions of Borough Councils with regard to sites for secondary schools before they are decided upon. Mixed Schools. 105. The subject of the desirability of mixed schools has again been considered, and the Council decided to call the attention of the London County Council to a report of H.M. Inspector upon the Haselrigge Road School, and to the resolution passed at this Council's Education Conference in October, 1905, and adopted by the Council, that in the 63 opinion of the Conference the policy of making the County Council schools mixed departments should be discontinued and separate departments established for boys and girls, and asked the London County Council to carry the resolution into effect. The Council was informed that its communication on the subject had been submitted to the Committee of the London County Council concerned. University Honours. 106. The Council addressed a communication to the Managers of the Haselrigge Road and Aristotle Road Schools, Clapham, congratulating Messrs. Harrison and Woodward, ex-scholars of the Haselrigge Road School, and the Managers and staff of that school, upon the success of Messrs. Harrison and Woodward at the Mathematical Tripos at the University of Cambridge. The Managers thanked the Council for its congratulations and stated that they would communicate the terms of the Council's letters to Messrs. Harrison and Woodward. Infantile Mortality. 107. The Council was represented at the Conference held at Westminster, on Infantile Mortality, by Alderman Gay and Councillor Mark Robinson—Chairman and Vice-Chairman respectively of the Public Health Committee. The President of the Local Government Board attended the Conference and gave an address. The Prime Minister afterwards received a deputation on the subject. Milk Snpply. 108. A Conference of the Public Health Committee and the registered milk vendors in the Borough was convened by the Council and held on July 17th, 1906, to consider the following subjects, viz. :— (1) What methods could be adopted to provide that the milk supply to London might be properly protected from contamination at the time of milking and during transit. (2) Any representations that might be made to the proper authorities, if necessary with the assistance of other local authorities in London, to ensure the adoption of such methods. (3) Any other methods or suggestions that might contribute to the cleanly distribution of milk in London. 109. At the Conference, which was attended by the Members of the Public Health Committee and by 54 registered dairymen 64 and keepers of milkshops in the Borough, the following resolutions were adopted, viz. :— (1) That the Board of Agriculture be urged to bring into force and carry out the Dairies, Cowsheds and Milkshops Order in the country, with such variations as are applicable in the various districts. (2) That the Board of Agriculture be requested to take steps to ensure that the udders of the cows, and the hands and arms of the person milking, shall be clean at the time of milking, and that the vessel used shall be clean and be kept solely for the purpose of milking. (3) That in the opinion of this Conference all Railway Companies carrying milk should provide proper insulated trucks, as now used by the London and North-Western Railway Company for the purpose. (4) That the Board of Agriculture be urged to take steps to ensure the carriage of milk in sealed churns. (5) That in the opinion of this Conference all dairies should be licensed annually, and only licensed dairies should sell milk for consumption on or off the premises, and that all milk on the counter for sale in open vessels should be covered with muslin or other suitable cover. (6) It was generally admitted that preservatives are not necessary. 110. With a view to the delivery of milk in London free from dirt a copy of the resolutions was sent to the Board of Agriculture and the other Metropolitan Borough and City Councils and they were asked, if they approved, to support such resolutions. Several of these Councils expressed their approval and communicated their views on the subject to the Government Department. 111. The Council was represented at a Conference held at Southwark on the 30th May, 1906, with reference to the sale of milk, warranties, etc., when the following resolutions were passed, viz. :— (a) That in the opinion of this Conference the provisions of Section 25 of the Sale of Food and Drugs Act, 1875, and Section 20 of the Food and Drugs Amendment Act, 1899, operate to the prejudice of the purchaser and the public health and should be repealed, leaving the seller of milk to his remedy at common law against the person from whom he purchases it. (b) That notices in shops, etc., to the effect that milk sold therein is not warranted as pure shall be no defence. (c) That the act of mixing water or separated milk with whole milk for the purpose of sale, shall be made a penal offence. (d) That any Metropolitan Borough Council should be enabled to sample at any railway station milk coming to dealers in the area of that Borough, although the station may be in another district. 65 (e) That copies of the foregoing resolutions be forwarded to the President of the Local Government Board and the President of the Board of Agriculture. Preserved Foods. 112. The following resolutions, which were passed by the Society of Medical Officers of Health on the subject of tinned and preserved foods, were considered by the Council, viz. :— That in the opinion of the Metropolitan Branch of the Incorporated Society of Medical Officers of Health, it is necessary, in order to secure better protection for the public in regard to tinned and preserved foods, that legislation should be passed embodying the following important reforms :— (1) That the name and address of the manufacturer and the date of canning should be impressed on the cans. (2) It shall be unlawful to sell for any purpose canned goods that are unfit for human consumption, and it should be the duty of any person in possession of unsound canned foods to notify and surrender same to the local authority, who should destroy such unsound food free of charge. (3) A certain number of tins (say one per cent.) from each consignment should be opened on importation and the contents examined under the supervision or instructions of the sanitary authority concerned prior to such canned foods being placed on the market. (4) Tins which are known in the trade as "doubtfuls" should be deemed to be unfit for human consumption. (5) Food intended for canning should be inspected by independent officials prior to being canned, and all foods should be canned under strictly hygienic conditions. (6) In the opinion of the Branch, the use of preservatives in canned food is unnecessary and should under no circumstances be permitted. 113. The Council were informed that such resolutions had been forwarded to the Local Government Board, the Board of Agriculture, the London County Council, and the City and Borough Councils. The Council agreed with the resolutions except with regard to the number of tins opened, which the Council was of opinion Should not be so great as one per cent., and communications were addressed to the Local Government Board, the Board of Agriculture, and the London County Council in support thereof. The Council also supported the suggestion of the Poplar Borough Council that local sanitary authorities should have the 66 same right of entry and of the institution of legal proceedings, in respect of factories where food is prepared, as is possessed by Factory Inspectors under Section 135 of the Factory and Workshop Act, 1901, and that the provisions affecting such factories should be extended to places where food is prepared other than factories. Infant Life Protection. 114. The Council considered the resolution, passed by the Society of Medical Officers of Health, with reference to the provisions of the Infant Life Protection Act, 1897, suggesting that the Act should be amended in order to provide for the control of persons receiving for hire or reward for a period of 48 hours one or more infants under the age of five years, and that in London the controlling authorities under the Act should be the Borough Councils. The Council agreed with the resolution, and a communication was addressed to the Local Government Board in support thereof. 115. Other information relating to public health is contained in the Medical Officer's Report, Appendix L. Openings in Roads. 116. The Borough Council has again had under consideration the inconvenience caused by the works carried out by different authorities having powers to break up streets. The London County Council having suggested that that Council should be empowered to make regulations dealing with the matter, the Borough Council decided not to object to the proposal, subject to the regulations being approved by a majority of the Borough Councils before confirmation, and to their administration being left entirely to the discretion of those Councils. The subject is still under the consideration of the County Council. Metropolitan Traffic; Board. 117. With reference to the report of the Royal Commission on London Traffic, the Hammersmith Borough Council urged that, in the event of the creation of a Metropolitan Traffic Board, the Borough Councils should be directly represented thereon by jointly-elected delegates; but this Council did not see its way to concur in the proposal. The Council was, however, in favour of the formation of a Traffic Board for London, consisting of not more than five nor less than three members, preferably the latter, as suggested by the 6 7 missioners, and asked the Government to promote a Bill in Parliament to give effect to this proposal. A deputation, arranged by the Browning Hall Conference on Housing, attended before the General Purposes Committee of the London County Council in July last, in support of steps being taken to secure the immediate appointment of a Traffic Board in accordance with the views of the Royal Commission. Councillor Walker represented this Council on the deputation. Motor Traffic. 118. A number of complaints having been received during the past year of the annoyance caused by motor omnibus traffic, the Council called the attention of the Commissioner of Police thereto, as the omnibuses are licensed by the police before being put on the roads. In reply to the Council's communications the Commissioner stated that he had given his special attention to the problems presented by the general introduction of motor omnibuses and was satisfied that the police were fully alive to the necessity of strictly enforcing the law in all cases, that whenever vapour was seen to be emitted by motor omnibuses the matter was reported by the police and particulars sent to the London County Council for any action to be taken which that Council might consider desirable; that in consequence of the action of the police in taking proceedings against drivers exceeding the 12 mile limit and also in communicating with the proprietors on the subject, the drivers were now more careful as to speed; that the attention of the proprietors had already been drawn to the practice which was beginning to obtain of repairing and oiling omnibuses at standings, and that although in a few cases oiling continued, repairing had ceased, and he (the Commissioner) had made representations to the proprietors as to the nuisance caused by the dropping of lubricating oil; and that the police were taking what steps they could to abate the nuisances complained of. The police have since been able to arrange for these omnibuses to be restricted to the main streets, as far as possible, and the annoyance to inhabitants of residential roads has thereby been considerably lessened. 119. A Conference convened by the Islington and Kensington Borough Councils with the view of joint action being taken to promote legislation for controlling the motor traffic of London was held at the Westminster City Hall in January last, this 68 Council being represented by Alderman Hewett, Councillors Law and Eastwood. The following resolutions were passed at the Conference and forwarded to the Home Secretary, the President of the Local Government Board, and the Commissioner of Police, viz. :— (1) That in the opinion of this Conference it is desirable that action should be taken without delay to secure a more effective control and supervision of the Motor Traffic in London, more particularly the heavy locomotives and motors now used for haulage purposes. (2) That the Commissioner of Police should be empowered, after consultation with the City and Borough Councils, to define the routes along which motor omnibuses should be allowed to run. (3) That the attention of the Commissioner of Police be called to the nuisance caused by the dropping of oil upon the roads by motor omnibuses, particularly at turning or stopping places, and that he be urged, before licensing these vehicles, to see that the same are provided with means to prevent grease or petrol dropping therefrom on to the roadways, and further to see that such means are duly used. (4) That in the opinion of this Conference it is incumbent upon the Commissioner of Police to exercise the greatest possible care in the licensing of drivers of motor omnibuses, in order that no unqualified driver may be employed, and that the safety of the public may be fully ensured. (5) That the Commissioner of Police should be empowered to prevent the use on the streets of any locomotives or traction engines which, either alone or with trailers, are unduly noisy, or otherwise unsuitable for road transport purposes, particularly during the night. (6) That it is desirable that all locomotives and trailers used in the streets should have proper springs, and that the tyres of the wheels should be shod with material as noiseless as possible. (7) That in the County of London the number of trailers to a locomotive should be reduced from three to two, exclusive of any wagon solely used for carrying water for the locomotive. (8) That copies of the resolutions passed by the Conference be forwarded to the Home Secretary, the President of the Local Government Board and the Commissioner of Police, with the request that the representations contained therein may receive their careful consideration, and that early effect may be given to the same by legislation or otherwise. (9) That communications be addressed to the Motor Van, Wagon and Omnibus Users' Association and other similar societies, and also to the City and Borough Councils, pointing out the desirability of their using india-rubber or other noiseless material on the tyres of the wheels of their motor vehicles. 69 120. The Council decided to concur in the resolutions Nos. 1 to 8, but did not see its way to agree with the resolution No. 9. A deputation, upon which Alderman Hewett represented this Council, subsequently waited upon the President of the Local Goverment Board and the Home Secretary in support of the views of the Conference. Streatham Tramway Extension. 121. The Borough Council has again had under consideration the subject of the suggested extension of the London County Council's tramways at Streatham. A new proposal has been made for the construction of a line of tramways from the Streatham terminus along Streatham High Road and Mitcham Lane to the county boundary at Roe Bridge so as to serve the neighbourhood of Mitcham Lane, where the population is rapidly increasing. As it was found that the extension of the tramway from the Tate Public Library to the county boundary at Norbury was not among the County Council's Parliamentary proposals for next year, the Borough Council, on the 31st July last, decided to ask the County Council to include in its applications to Parliament next Session, the scheme proposed by the County Council in 1902, for a double line of tramways along Streatham High Road and the widening of that thoroughfare to 60 feet in connection therewith, the Borough Council reserving its right to oppose the Bill containing the scheme. Both proposals are under the consideration of the Highways Committee. Wandsworth to Putney Tramways. 122. The Borough Council had under consideration a scheme, proposed by the London County Council, for widening Upper Richmond Road and East Hill, Wandsworth, and the formation of a new road from Buckhold Road to Broomhill Road, in connection with the suggested construction of tramways from the existing tramway terminus at East Hill, via East Hill, High Street, Wandsworth, West Hill, Upper Richmond Road, and High Street, Putney, to join the authorised tramways at the south side of Putney Bridge, the existing tramways in North Street, York Road and Red Lion Street forming a loop, and other loop lines being provided via Buckhold Road, the proposed new road, and Broomhill Road, and via Oxford Road and Putney Bridge Road, so as to avoid further extensive street widenings. The London County Council asked whether, in the event of the improvement being undertaken, the Borough Council would 70 give its consent to the tramways, and to the reduction, where necessary, in the width of the footways, and would contribute onethird of the nett cost (estimated at £59,250) of the necessary street widenings. In reply the County Council was informed that the Borough Council was of opinion that, in view of the proposed construction of the tramways, the widening of the thoroughfare from Lambeth to Putney advocated by the Royal Commission on London Traffic as an arterial street improvement—the only such improvement recommended south of the Thames—should be carried out at once, and a double line of tramways constructed in High Street, Wandsworth, in lieu of the loop lines proposed by the County Council; that the construction of a single line of electric tramway in Fairfield Street and High Street would render those thoroughfares very dangerous, the awkward curves at the junction of the roads in the proposed loop line from High Street along Fairfield Street and York Road back to High Street being also very objectionable, and the Borough Council suggested that from High Street, Wandsworth, a double line of tramways should be laid along Putney Bridge Road to High Street, Putney, and thence to Putney Bridge in lieu of the proposed line along Upper Richmond Road, and that Putney Bridge Road should be widened where necessary for the purpose. The Borough Council also submitted to the County Council an alternative plan for the widening of East Hill. Having further considered the matter the Highways Committee of the County Council decided not to proceed with the proposals at present, but expressed the opinon that in view of the importance of the lines in connection with the development of the existing tramways system with which they would be connected, arrangements should be made for their construction as soon as the County Council was in a position to deal with the matter. Wandsworth Road and East Hill Tramways. 123. With reference to the proposed re-construction for electric traction of the tramways in Wandsworth Road, Lavender Hill, St. John's Hill, and East Hill, the London County Council informed the Borough Council in July last, that, owing to the additional street widenings asked for by the Wandsworth and Battersea Borough Councils, the clauses relating to the matter had to be withdrawn from the County Council's Tramways and Improvements Bill of the present Session, that the Highways Committee of the County 71 Council were impressed with the desirability of re-constructing these lines at an early date, and hoped that this Council would be willing to consider some modification of the proposals which it previously put forward, and suggested that, with the view of arriving at an agreement, representatives of the two Borough Councils should meet a few members of the Highways Committee to discuss the matter. The Borough Council consented to take part in the proposed Conference, and the Mayor, Aldermen Lorden and Parker, and Councillor Dawnay were appointed as this Council's representatives. Mitcham Road and Tooting High Street Tramways. 124. In March last, the Borough Council was informed that the London County Council proposed to adopt an underground conduit system of electrical traction for the tramways to be constructed in Mitcham Road, from Tooting Broadway to Tooting Junction railway station, and in Tooting High Street (late Merton Road) from Tooting Broadway to Longley Road, and had applied to the Board of Trade for its consent. This consent has been given; the Borough Council and the County Council have entered into agreements with respect to the necessary widening of the road and the paving works, and the construction of the tramway is now in hand. 125. The London United Tramways Company have constructed a tramway on the overhead trolley system of electrical traction in the portion of Tooting High Street (formerly Merton Road), transferred to this Borough under the recent alteration of the county boundary at Tooting. The powers to construct the line were obtained by the Company before the alteration took place. Tooting High Street Widening. 126. The whole of the property required for widening Tooting High Street (late Merton Road) for the proposed electric tramways from Tooting Broadway to Longley Road having been acquired by the London County Council the widening of the public way has been carried out and the construction of the tramway and the paving works are in progress. E 72 York Road Widening. 127. In pursuance of the agreement with the London County Council, the Borough Council has been pressing forward the negotiations for the acquisition of property needed for widening York Road, Wandsworth, in connection with the re-construction of the tramways there for electrical traction. In a number of cases the claims of the owners have been settled and the land thrown into the public way and paved. Backshaw Road Improvement. 128. The whole of the land required for widening the narrow part of Blackshaw Road between the Lambeth Cemetery and Tooting High Street has been acquired and thrown into the public way. A new sewer has been constructed and the work of paving the road is now in progress. Approach to River Wandle at High Street, Wandsworth. 129. In accordance with the agreement referred to in last year's report, an order has been obtained for stopping-up the approach to the River Wandle at High Street, Wandsworth. Putney Bridge Road Widening. 130. All the property needed for the widening of Putney Bridge Road, near Wandsworth Park, having been acquired, the forecourt walls have been set back ana tne paving works executed. The Borough Council has again been in communication with the London County Council with regard to the proportion of the cost of the various improvements in Putney Bridge Road to be defrayed out of the county fund, but the County Council decided to adhere to the view that a complete scheme of widening should be prepared and submitted before the question of contribution is dealt with. The subject of obtaining the required information is now under the consideration of the Borough Council. Widening at Coiner 01 Upper Richmond Road and Putney Hill. 131. The work of erecting the new London and County Bank at the corner of Upper Richmond Road and Putney Hill having been completed, the land in front has been added to the public way and the necessary paving works executed. The London County Council only propose to contribute one-third of the cost of the improvement, and the Borough Council is in communication with the County Council with the view of obtaining an increased contribution. 73 Putney Bridge Convenience. 132. In connection with the widening of the approach to Putney Bridge from Lower Richmond Road for the proposed electric tramways from Putney to Hammersmith, the Borough Council arranged for a sanitary convenience to be constructed beneath the approach. The work is now being carried out by the London County Council, and the Local Government Board has sanctioned the raising of a loan to defray the cost of the convenience, which will be borne by the Borough Council. Lower Richmond Road Widening and Woodpaving. 133. The Highways Committee have on several occasions had before them the question of the acquisition of property needed for the widening of Lower Richmond Road. In view, however, of the excessive cost which would be incurred by the Council in dealing with some of the premises affected, the Committee did not see their way to recommend the Council to take further action in the matter at present. The Committee hope that a favourable opportunity will occur shortly for completing the improvement, and the subject will continue to receive their attention. 134. The Council has again had under consideration the subject of the desirability of paving the carriageway of Lower Richmond Road with wood, and on the 17th July last two deputations from Putney attended before the Council and urged that immediate steps should be taken with the view of the work being carried out irrespective of any tramway or widening scheme. The London County Council is pressing forward the alterations to the approach to Putney Bridge required for the construction of the authorised tramways from Hammersmith to Putney Bridge, one of the termini of which will be at the eastern end of Lower Richmond Road, and it appeared to the Borough Council that the County Council may desire to extend these tramways to the county boundary on Barnes Common. The widenings already approved, and those which will be necessary in the event of the road becoming a tramway route, will involve certain alterations of the levels, and these could be effected at the least cost by carrying out the paving works at the same time as the widening of the road. Under these circumstances the Borough Council came to the conclusion that it would be advisable, before definitely settling the question of paving, to ascertain whether the London County 74 Council proposes to formulate a scheme for the provision of tramways along Lower Richmond Road, and they are in communication with the County Council on the subject. 135. The rule relating to the times of funerals at the Council's cemeteries has been amended so as to provide that the Parish, Asylum, and Prison funerals shall take place at the following fixed times, viz. At half-past three o'clock during the months of November to February, and at a quarter-past four o'clock during the months of March to October, the Council being of opinion that these times would be more suitable to the relatives of the deceased than twelve o'clock noon, the time at which these funerals usually took place previously. Fees, Charges, Rules and Regulations. 140. The book of fees, charges, rules, and regulations in force at the Council's cemeteries has been revised, and application is being made for the approval, where necessary, of the Local Government Board to the proposed amendments. The principal alterations are in the rules relating to the fees to be charged in respect of non-parishioners, and for the re-opening of graves. The planting and turfing fees were also revised, and some of the charges reduced. Claim for Burial Fees. 141. Contractors. 150. A leaden box, provided by the contractor, was placed beneath the stone containing a copy of the Council's last annual report, minutes of the Council, copies of The Times, Wandsworth Borough News, Streatham News, Balham Weekly Mail, Clapham Observer, Tooting Parish Magazine—which contained an account of the Old Vestry Hall—signed photographs of the Mayor (Councillor Glegg, J.F.) and Alderman Anderson, and the following coins :—Crown, half-crown, florin, shilling, sixpenny-piece, threepenny-piece, penny, halfpenny, and farthing. 151. The architect presented to Alderman Anderson a trowel commemorating the ceremony. Admission of Members of Swimming Clubs to tne Baths. 152. The Council considered the subject of the admission of members of clubs to the baths at reduced rates, and it was decided that in future only regularly organised swimming clubs, having committees and other officers, and proper rules for their management, should be placed upon the list of approved clubs. Notices were sent to each club, informing them of the Council's decision and requiring them to comply with the new conditions. In the majority of cases the conditions have been complied with and the clubs have been placed upon the list. 78 APPORTIONMENTS. 153. HENRY GEORGE HILLS, Town Clerk. East Hill, Wandsworth, S.W., August, 1907. 113 Appendix " A." REPORT OF SURVEYORS. MILEAGE OF ROADS. Garratt Lane, has been taken up and a 9-inch pipe sewer with two manholes constructed in lieu thereof, at a cost of ₤72 14s. 7d. The New Streets Committee, considering that this road was sufficiently built upon, decided to make up the widened Mount Ephraim Road portion as a new street. It was necessary before taking over as a highway to lay a new sewer to take the surface water from the roadway, as none existed. A 9-inch pipe sewer surrounded with concrete has been laid, at a cost of ₤125 4s. 3d. 116 During the heavy rains which have fallen during the summer Ravcnslea months flooding has occurred in this road at a point near Road- the Battersea boundary In order to prevent the sewage backing up into private premises two flap valves have been fixed in the Council's sewer, which have so far prevented a recurrence of this trouble. The 12-inch pipe sewer has been extended from opposite "Langley" West Hill. to the west side of " Woodlands" and a manhole con- structed, at a cost of ₤210 3s. 9d. The owner of " Woodlands " contributed ₤50 towards the cost. A new 18-inch pipe sewer with four manholes has been constructed Wimbledon on the site of a suggested new road in extension of Park Estate. Granville Road, Wandsworth, to the western boundary of Melrose Gardens, at a cost of ₤382 15s. 1d.. The owners of land through which the sewer passes contributed the sum of ₤242 towards the cost. Sanction has been given by the Local Government Board for the Underground construction of Underground Conveniences for both sexes stottontoad' Station Road, Balham, a description of which was Balham. given in last year's Report. Tenders were invited for the work, and that of Mr. E. Wall, Alice Terrace, Summerstown, S.W., of ₤1,790, was accepted. A separate contract was entered into for the supply and fixing of Sanitary Fittings, and the tender of Messrs. G. & D. Musgrave, Ltd., St. Martin's Lane, YV.C., of £106 6s. 2d., was accepted for this work. The roof of the Convenience now forms a very useful refuge, the traffic at this point being very heavy. The work was commenced on July 31st, 1906, and the Convenience was opened to the public on February 13th, 1907. Conveniences under Railway Arch Balham Station. Wall, of Summerstown. The total cost of this work Station. was ₤14 2s. od. Owing to the new Underground Convenience being open the above Convenience under the Railway Arch has been closed, but it will be open on Bank Holidays, Race days, and special occasions. Searle & Searle, acting on behalf of the owners of Rastrick House, the property adjoining the library, by exchanging a piece of land belonging to the library for a strip of land belonging 132 to Rastrick House on the west side of the building; in consideration of such exchange the owners of such house have agreed to build a new wall dividing the properties from end to end and to build a new lavatory on the library premises in place of the old lavatory, which will be broken into by the wall. General Report on Testings of Gas supplied to the Wandsworth Borough Council (1) by the Wandsworth and Putney Gas Company; (2) by the Mitcham and Wimbledon Gas Company, for the year ended 31ST March, 1907. Another year has elapsed since my general report on the daily testings of gas supplied by this Company from October, 1903, to March, 1906, and it may therefore be of interest to review the results of the continued daily testings to March, 1907. W an d s worth and Putney Gas Company. The standard illuminating power of the gas supplied by this Company is 14 candles. The average illuminating power for the twelve months ended March 31st, 1907, is 14.7 candles; the gas has mainly fluctuated between 14.5 and 15.0 candles, as will be clearly seen from the curve which 1 have constructed from the daily testings (Table 3). Thus, while the illuminating power was somewhat more uniform than in the corresponding periods 1904-05 and 1905-06, the general average is somewhat lower than during those two periods, which showed an average of 15.2 and 15.5 respectively. Table 1 shows the monthly averages of the illuminating power for 1906-07, calculated from daily testings. Illuminating Power. On four occasions during this period I found the gas below the standard illuminating power, viz. :—13.6 candles on October 20th, 1906, and 12.8, 13.6 and 13.9 candles respectively on January 23rd and 24th and March 19th, 1907. Previous to July, 1906, the sulphur impurities other than sulphuretted hydrogen were not allowed to exceed 20 grains in 100 cubic feet of gas, but all tions as to these sulphur impurities were abolished by the Removal of Sulphur Restrictions Act, July 20th, 1906, and forfeitures had already been temporarily suspended for some months previously by the Council at the request of the Company. By this Act, however, by special arrangement with the Company, testing for sulphur impurities other than sulphuretted hydrogen has been continued. Notwithstanding the abolition of forfeitures Sulphur. 146 and the withdrawal of restrictions by Act of Parliament as to the amount of sulphur present in the gas, the Company have very seldom exceeded 30 grains in 100 cubic feet of gas during the twelve months ended 31st March, 1907; the amount has fluctuated between 22 and 27 grains, as will be seen from the curve which I have constructed from the daily testings (Table 3), and the average for the whole period was 24.6 grains. In Table 1 will be seen the monthly averages of the sulphur impurity calculated from the daily testings. 1 still note the somewhat remarkable relation to which 1 referred in my last report (Report of Council for the year ended 31st March, 1906, p. 163) between the amount of sulphur and the illuminating power, and a distinct tendency towards an inverse ratio between the two, as will be seen from a comparison of the two curves in Table 3. There has been no sulphuretted hydrogen in the gas during the year ended 31st March, 1907. Sulphuretted Hydrogen. This has rarely been less than a pressure equal to a column of two inches of water, that is to say, more than one inch above the statutory minimum; daily observations have been made and duly reported. Pressure. The only legislation relating to the Wandsworth and Putney Gas Company during the year ended 31st March, 1907, has been the passing of the Gas Companies (Removal of Sulphur Restrictions) Act, 1906, which came into force in July last, and to which I have already alluded above under the heading "Sulphur." I have also made two reports on this Act, one when it was in the Bill stage on January 10th, 1906, and one on December 13th, 1906, some time after the passing of the Act. The main points to be rioted in connection with the gas testing and to which I have previously called attention are (1) the continuance of the testing for sulphur impurities other than sulphuretted hydrogen, (2) testing the illuminating power with flat flame burner, (3) testing the calorific power of the gas, '(4) extension by one hour in advance of the usual times of testing under the Gas Works Clauses Act, 1871. At present the Council do not propose to carry out (2) and (3), but the Act enables them to make these tests at any time. Becent Legislation. 147 TABLE I. Peppercorn. 264 265 Appendix "J." EXTRACT FROM REPORT OF DISTRESS COMMITTEE. A Sub-Committee was tormed, constituted as follows :—Mr. Percival Rees (Chairman); Councillors Clark, Cresswell, Danielli, Fletcher, and Jackson, Lieut. Sanders, R.N., Rev. J. Cooney, Mr. Fowle, and Miss Parkes, and the Chairman and Members of the Central Body, ex-officio; the duties being to enquire and report as to the conditions of labour, to consider the Record Papers when completed, and to interview men selected for employment and emigration. Sub-Committee and reference. Applications from unemployed persons were received daily at Reception at Office. the Town Hall, Wandsworth, during the winter months, between the hours of 9 a.m. and 12 o'clock noon. The particulars entered on the Record Papers were investi- Investigation. gated and verined by personal visits to the homes of the applicants by the investigators, and references as to character were obtained from previous employers. No personal investigations were made outside the Borough. The Sub-Committee considered the completed Record Papers Classification. and classified the applications into four divisions:— Preference, Eligible, Unsuitable and Undesirable. In recommending applicants to the Central Body as suitable for employment, the Sub-Committee gave preference to men of good character, and distinguished, as far as possible, between the habitual "out of work" and the applicant who was unemployed through trade depression or kindred causes. The forms of the applicants for emigration were carefully con- Emigration. sidered before being forwarded to the Central Body, and single men were asked to appear before the Sub-Committee in order to judge of their suitability, and the responsibility of their guarantors. The wages of the men from this Borough, employed on the London County Council and Royal Parks, were paid on Saturday morning at the Town Hall, Payment of wages. 266 Wandsworth, the week's work being from Monday morning to Friday night. The rate of wages was 6d. per hour. The men sent to the Working Colonies receive 6d. per week Family allowance. in addition to their board and lodging, a weekly allowance being made to the family on the following scale:—ios. to the wife, 2s. for the first child under 14, 1s. 6d. for the second child under 14, and 1s. for each other child under 14 years; but in no case was 17s. 6d. to be exceeded. The allowances are paid at the homes of the men on Tuesdays. The men are allowed a furlough at the end of each month, their fares being paid for them. Volunteers. The Committee has not availed itself of the services of additional voluntary workers. Effect of the provision of work. The effect produced upon those who obtained work, through the medium of the Distress Committee, varied according to the character of the applicant. In the case of those anxious to work and keep their homes together it was noticeable that after a week's wages, when nourishing food had been obtained, they gradually improved in efficiency. Advantages of Colony work. Colony work has the advantage of fresh air and healthy surroundings, and of removing men from many local temptations. For various reasons, a number of applicants have declined to leave their homes for any period, preferring to wait for local employment. Experience in Administering the Act. In administering the Act, the Committee found reason to believe that a false impression was created amongst the unemployed, who, after having had the enquiry forms filled up and having received visits from the Investigators as to the correctness of the statements, felt that work was certain. As only 214 out of 961 applicants obtained about seven weeks' work, the disappointed balance lost confidence in the usefulness of the Act, and to some extent also in those administering it. The impression that work was coming also slackened their efforts to obtain employment elsewhere. Methods of Payment. The Committee are of opinion that the best method of paying allowances is that at present in force, viz., at the homes of the men, where assistance and advice can 267 be given when necessary. The Committee have not advocated visits from voluntary workers to the homes of the men employed on the Colonies, preferring to rely upon the official reports of the Clerk and the Investigators. Value of Conferences. The Committee are of opinion that Distress Committee Conferences are of very little practical utility. Value of the "Reserve" System. The system of having a number of "reserve" preference cases in addition to the actual number of vacancies allotted to the various works has been of great advantage, and much time is thus saved in filling vacancies as they occur. Preliminary Investigation. The Committee had referred to them, before investigation, all cases where there appeared to be a technical disqualification, and if the applicant did not comply with the regulations issued by the Local Government Board no further action was taken. All other applications were fully investigated. Apprenticeship. As the information necessary to answer this question was not asked for in the schedule of instructions received from the Local Government Board it cannot be given. It, however, may be of interest to know that a Committee on Industrial Education, with Dr. Macnamara as Chairman, is now sitting considering the apprenticeship question. This Committee has collected a vast amount of very useful information bearing on apprenticeship which the Central Body ought to obtain. There are two and only two possible remedies for the distress Unemployed. of the unemployed. The first is some change of policy which will create, on sound commercial lines, fresh sources of industrial activity. The other and sad alternative is the deportation of these workmen to countries where better conditions for prosperity are made to prevail. ALEX. GLEGG, Chairman. PERCIVAL REES, V ice-Chairman. Metropolitan Borough of Wandsworth. REPORTS OF Libraries Sub-Committees. 279 Clapham Public Library. TWENTIETH ANNUAL REPORT: 1st April, 1906, to 31st March, 1907. SUB-COMMITTEE, 1907. Alderman H. BULCRAIG (Chairman). Alderman J. G. KIPLING. Councillor J. BUSFIELD. 'Councillor C. COLMAN. Councillor J. W. CUNDALL. Councillor H. G. DOWNER. 'Councillor R. KENT. Councillor J. LIDIARD. REED WELCH. 8 280 ANNUAL REPORT OF THE SUB-COMMITTEE OF CLAPHAM PUBLIC LIBRARY, For the Year ended 31st March, 1907. The Sub-Committee have pleasure in presenting the Twentieth Annual Report on the work of the Clapham Library. The number of books issued during the year was 94,722 in the Lending Library and 4,874 in the Reference Library (exclusive of books in open cases), being a total issue of 99,596 volumes, and a daily average issue of 412 volumes. These figures show an increase in the Lending Library issues of 493 volumes over the previous year, when the library was open on two more days than in 1906-7. Many of the books most in request in the Reference Library are placed on open shelves, and of the use made of these books no statistics are available. The record of issues of volumes in this department is therefore limited to those for which written applications are made, the number of which is less this year owing to a larger and better selection being placed in the open cases. The number of books now in stock, after allowing for withdrawal of worn-out and obsolete works, is 15,293, of which 12,033 are in the Lending Library and 3,260 in the Reference Library. 3,644 readers' tickets (including renewals) were issued during the year to borrowers ill the Lending Library. On the recommendation of the Clapham Sub-Committee the Council decided that any ratepayer of the Borough of Wandsworth should be eligible to sign as guarantor the application voucher forms of non-ratepaying readers in the Lending Libraries. The exterior wood and iron work of the library building has been re-painted during the year, and several internal structural alterations and improvements effected. 281 On the 1st January, 1907, at the St. Albans Quarter Sessions, a person was sentenced to one month's imprisonment with hard labour, for stealing Kelly's Handbook to the Tilled and Official Classes from the Reference Library. By order of the Council the blacking out of betting news in the newspapers placed in the Reading Rooms has been discontinued. The Sub-Committee desire to express their cordial thanks to the various donors of books, periodicals, etc., during the past year. The Borough Council in November, 1906, appointed Aldermen Bulcraig and Kipling, and Councillors Cundall, Downer, Lidiard, Monday and Richardson, as the Sub-Committee for the Clapham Library for 1907. Alderman Bulcraig was re-elected chairman, and a vote of thanks passed to him for his services in that capacity during the previous year. No alteration has been made in the staff, and the Sub-Committee again have pleasure in reporting their satisfaction of the efficient services of the Librarian and his staff of assistants. On behalf of the Sub-Committee, HENRY BULCRAIG, Chairman. 6th May, 1907. 282 NUMBER OF VOLUMES IN STOCK on 31ST MARCH, 1907, and VOLUMES ISSUED from ist APRIL, 1906, to 3ist MARCH, 1907. BRADLEY. 288 REPORT OF THE PUTNEY LIBRARY SUB-COMMITTEE, For the Year ended March 31st, 1907. The Sub-Committee in submitting the Nineteenth Annual Report of the Putney Library are again pleased to record a considerable growth in the various branches of its work. In December, 1906, the Borough Council appointed Alderman Parker, Councillors Hoile, Jackson and Radford as the Sub-Committee for 1907. At the first meeting of the Sub-Committee Alderman Parker was elected Chairman for the year. The issue of the Quarterly List of additions to the Library has been continued; this publication has, doubtless, been helpful to the many readers. The question of further protection of the Library from fire has been discussed, and as a means to this end, extra lengths of fire hose have been purchased at a cost of £3 16s. The Finance Committee passed estimates of expenditure amounting to ,£1,103, and during the year this sum has been expended. The Sub-Committee regret to report the death of the Rev. Stephen Todd, who was for many years intimately connected with the management of the institution, and they desire to place on record their appreciation of his devoted services. Owing to very heavy rain storms during the early part of the summer the basement of the building was flooded. During the period under review the following works were carried out under the supervision of the Surveyor:—The decoration of the main corridor, news room and lending department, works for the prevention of a recurrence of the floods in the basement, and the provision of a set of stone steps for better exit from the basement. These improvements were made at a cost of £76. 289 The best thanks of the Sub-Committee are tendered to the donors to the Library. A list of donors will be found on page 294 of this report. Lending Department. During the year 1906-7 there has been an increase in the stock this department of 662 volumes, of these 647 were added by purchase and 15 were presented to the Library. The total number of volumes now available for home reading amounts to 9,270. There has been an increase in the issues from the department of 3,356, the total issues for the year being 67,736, as compared with 64,380 volumes issued during the year 1905-6. Considerable attention has been paid to the old stock. During the year 410 volumes were rebound, 380 have been replaced, and 29 volumes have been withdrawn from the stock as not being worth replacing. The books in this department are shelved in open cases and Reference readers have free access to the books, a privilege Department, which is much appreciated by the residents of Putney. The Reference Library is also used by many persons residing in neighbouring districts. No statistics of the issues of books are available, but approximately 150 persons use the department daily. During the year, 96 volumes have been added, making the total stock at present 2,203 volumes. Magazine Rooms. Early in the year, with a view of relieving the crowded state of the Magazine room, the Sub-Committee decided that the spare room should be used as an additional magazine room. Judging by the use made of these two rooms the action of the Sub-Committee has been fully justified. Thirty-one monthly magazines and reviews, three quarterlies, and 40 weekly periodicals are placed in these rooms. The magazines and reviews are transferred to the lending department at the end of the month, and issued to borrowers for home reading. News Room. The list of newspapers, &c., provided in this room includes 28 daily newspapers, among which are Birmingham, Edinburgh, Liverpool, Manchester and Plymouth daily journals, 55 weekly newspapers and periodicals and 40 monthy and quarterly publications. 290 The Sub-Committee have much pleasure in expressing their appreciation of the satisfactory manner in which the Librarian and his staff have discharged their duties. Tables of information relating to the issues of books, classification, &c., will be found on pages 291-293. For list of newspapers, &c., see pages 295-296. Callarp. 293 REPORT OF THE SUB-COMMITTEE, 19061907. The Sub-Committee for the Streatham, Balham and Tooting Libraries have pleasure in submitting their report for the year ended 31st March, 1907. The number of volumes issued to borrowers was 361,935,. being a daily average of 1,502. This is the highest aggregate issue for one year since the establishment of the libraries. The figures of the previous year were 353,061. The following is a statement of the number of volumes circulated at each library. Lending Library. For reference purposes. Total. The Tate Library, Streatham 149-439 5630 155,069 Balham Public Library 137.862 604 138,466 Tooting Public Library 67,610 790 68,400 354,911 7,024 361,935 Compared with the previous year's statistics these figures show an increased issue of 8,250 volumes at the Tate Library and 5,853. at the Tooting Library. The issues at the Balham Library were 5,229 fewer than last year. 299 CLASSIFICATION OF BOOKS ISSUED. Class. Th- Tate Library, Streatham. Balham Library. Tooting Library. Total. A. Special attention has been given during the year to the revision . of the stock in the scientific and economic sections of the Tate Library, and many important works have been acquired. After deducting replacements, and books worn out and withdrawn from circulation, the stock at the 31st March numbered 34,503' volumes, distributed as follows:— The Tate Library, Streatham 17,528 Balham Public Library 10,692 Tooting Public Library 6,283 Total .34,503 At the annual stocktaking in July only two volumes (value 6s. 6d.) could not be accounted for. A.—Theology, Philosophy, etc. 1,232 538 212 1,982 B.—History and Biography 3,691 2,042 1,208 6,941 C.—Travels and Topography 1,549 967 475 2,991 D.—Art, Science, etc. 2,572 1,220 651 4.443 E.—Prose Fiction 3.750 3,505 1,989 9.244 F.—Poetry and the Drama 860 257 242 1.359 G.—Social Science, etc. 889 550 215 1.654 H.—Miscellaneous Literature 1,247 613 443 2,302 J.—Juvenile Literature 1,028 895 541 2,465 Books of Reference 710 104 308 1,122 Total 17,528 10,692 6,283 34,503 The preparation of catalogues has occupied a good deal of time and attention during the past year. In June, an Author-List of the books in the Tooting Library was published, and the printing has just been completed of a new Subject Catalogue of the books in the Tate Library relating to Science, Art, Sociology, etc. The Subcommittee hope to issue in the course of the .ensuing year a new catalogue of the books contained in the Balham Library, and catalogus of the Fiction and Juvenile departments at the Streatham Library. The usual lists of additious to the libraries have been issued during the year as well as those relating to topics of the day and other matters of public and local interest. The Reading Rooms are regularly supplied with a comprehensive selection of the leading newspapers and periodicals. The rooms are used extensively by the inhabitants of the district and are open every weekday from 9 a.m. to 10 p.m., and on Sundays (except during the months of July, August and September) from 3 to 9 p.m. 302 A list of the papers and magazines provided at each library is appended. See pages 307-312. The following is a statement of the receipts from the sale of catalogues, etc., and fines for the overdue detention of books. £ s. d. The Tate Library, Streatham 119 1 3 Balham Public Library 78 16 3 Tooting Public Library 46 9 4 £244 6 10 The Sub-Committee endeavour each year to renovate, wholly or in part, one of the buildings under their charge. Last summer the whole of the Tooting Library was re-painted and decorated. A list of the Donors who have kindly presented books, etc., to the libraries during the past year is given 011 pages 305 and 306. The rapid development now taking place in the district of Tooting and the increasing use that is being made of the Library (the generous gift of Sir William J. Lancaster, J.P., L.C.C.) will necessitate at an early date an extension of the building. The accommodation for books in the Lending Library is becoming inadequate for the requirements of the neighbourhood, and it is expected that by the end of the present year all the available space in this department will be taken up. More room for the public is also needed in front of the counter to relieve the crowding which occurs on busy evenings, making it somewhat difficult for readers to refer to the indicator or choose their books from the show-cases. The Reading Room is likewise very largely attended, and though accommodati-11 has been provided for about 90 readers as many as 100 persons are, occasionally, to be found in the Room at one time. At the Tate Library the increasing demand for works of reference is an indication that the establishment of a separate room for reference library purpjses cannot long be delayed. Last year the number of books consulted at the Streatham Library was 5,630 as distinct from those issued for home reading. 303 These are matters which will shortly need the careful consideration of the Libraries Committee if the Institutions are to be maintained in a state of efficiency. This Sub-Committeee have pleasure in expressing their appreciation of the satisfactory and efficient manner in which the Librarian and his staff have discharged their duties. Signed on behalf of the Sub-Committee. J. H. ANDERSON, Chairman. 6th May, 1907. 304 ANNUAL ISSUES AND STOCK Since the opening of the Libraries. Year. No. of Volumes in Stock. GATLAND. 314 SUB-COMMITTEE REPORT, For the Year ended 31st March, 1907. The three Libraries under the charge of this Sub-Committee, the West Hill Library, the Allfarthing Lane Branch Library and that for exchange of books, at Earlsfield, have been maintained during the year with increased usefulness. The cost of the Earlsfield Branch, which had been defrayed by Alderman Lorden for the previous two years, fell upon this SubCommittee, the Council having decided to continue the Branch as before. Mr. G. R. Gatland has been appointed Librarian-in-charge at the Allfarthing Lane Branch vice Mr. A. Nash, who resigned in March, 1906; Mr. L. H. Cousins, Senior Assistant at the West Hill Library, has passed the Library Association Examination for Cataloguing; and Mr. A. E. Macmillan has been made Saturday Assistant in place of Mr. T. Davis, who has resigned. The Librarian attended the Annual Conference of Librarians at Bradford and submitted his report 011 the same. The Library Assistants' Association met at the West Hill Library 011 May 16th, 1906. Mr. John Bell, J.P., a member of the Wandsworth Library Commission from its commencement in 1883 till its absorption by the Borougli Council in 1900, and for many years Chairman, died in October. This Committee sent a letter of condolence to his family. A burglary took place at West Hill, on December 21st—22nd, when £1 was stolen and some damage done. The loss has been met by the Commercial Union Assurance Company. The springs of the swing doors in the Longstaff Reading Room have been leplaced by "Slave" springs. 3i5 "Blacking out" of betting news in the newspapers has been discontinued by order of the Council. It will be seen from the accompanying tables that the issue of books in the lending department has largely increased in the last year, and that since the Council took over the libraries, seven years ago, the increase has been from 83,953 to 124,979 or nearly 50 per cent. The books issued in the reference department at West Hill numbered 4,299, exclusive of those in open cases, as compared with 2,510 last year. The Quarterly List has been issued regularly, and in it have appeared the additions to the West Hill Library from 1903 to 1905; a Fiction and Juvenile Author List of books in the Allfarthing Lane Branch Library; and a List of the books in the Branch for the exchange of books, Eailsfield. To the Mayor, Aldermen and Councillors of the Metropolitan Borough of Wandsworth. Sir and Gentlemen, I have the honour to present the Annual Report of the health and sanitary condition of the Borough for the year 1906. Population. The population of the Borough has not been estimated by the method advised by the Registrar General as, in consequence of the rapid growth of the Borough, such method would, in my opinion, give fallacious results. The population has been estimated in a similar manner to that adopted in 1905, by dividing the number of inhabitants at the Census of 1901 by the number of rated hereditaments in that year, thus obtaining a factor representing the number of persons per hereditament, by which the number of hereditaments in the middle of 1906 is multiplied. The factor thus obtained does not vary very much from year to year, and, consequently, the estimated population is nearer the truth than the population estimated by any other available method. Table I. shows the population of each Ward and the number of hereditaments in 1901, and the number of hereditaments in the middle of 1906 and the estimated population. A 6 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. TABLE I. Ward. Census Population, 1901. Rated Hereditaments, 1901. Rated Hereditaments, 1906. Estimated Population, 1906. Increase since 1901. Clapham, North 28,772 5,081 5,574 31,540 2,768 „ South 22,589 4,115 6,361 34,980 12,391 Putney 24,139 4,553 5,581 29,570 5,431 Streatham 33,624 6.759 9,359 46,750 13,126 Balham 38,034 7,204 9,206 48,500 10,466 Tooting 16,473 3,341 6,210 30,610 14,137 Southfield 21,924 3.963 6,492 35,950 14,026 Fairfield 18,520 3,896 4,058 19,280 760 Springfield 27,959 4,384 6,085 38,820 10,861 Whole Borough 232,034 43,296 58,926 316,000 83,966 Table II. shows the population of each sub-district, compared with the Census population, uncorrected and corrected for Institutions. Clapham 51,361 66,800 66,520 Putney 24,139 29,500 29,570 Streatham 71,658 94,750 95,250 Tooting 16,473 28,610 30,6lO Wandsworth 68,403 90,650 94,050 Whole Borough 232,034 310,310 316,000 7 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Table I. shows that since 1901 there has been a very great increase in all the Wards with the exception of Fairfield, where it has only been very slight. The estimated population corrected for Institutions in 1905 was 285,910, so that there has been in one year an estimated increase of 24,400, while the rated hereditaments have increased from 54,870 to 58,926. Since 1901 the increase has been 83,966, and this increase has been greatest in Tooting, Southfield, and Streatham, and least in Clapham North and Fairfield Wards. The corrected population in Table II. is used to calculate the rates in this Report. The difference between the corrected and uncorrected population is made up by the population in the Public Institutions of the Borough, which are as follows:— Clapham:—Hostel of God. Putney:—The Priory, Roehampton. Streatham:—St. Anne's Home. (St. Pancras parish.) Middlesex County Lunatic Asylum (part of). Tooting:—Tooting Home. Metropolitan Asylums Board's Hospitals— The Fountain and the Grove. Newlands Asylum. Tooting Bee Asylum. Wandsworth:—The Workhouse. Royal Hospital for Incurables. Northlands Home for Inebriates. Middlesex County Lunatic Asylum (part of). H.M. Prison. The total population of these Institutions was about 6,300, but as a considerable proportion of the inmates of the Tooting Home, the Workhouse, and the Metropolitan Asylums Board's Hospitals belong to other Boroughs, a deduction of 5,690 made 8 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. from the uncorrected total population gives approximately the corrected population, viz.:—310,310, compared with 285,910 in 1905, and 264,200 in 1904. There are very few Boroughs in the Metropolis where the increase of population has been so marked, and if the present rate of increase continues the population of the Borough at the next Census will be nearly 400,000. Births. During the 52 weeks ending the 29th December, 1906, 7,057 births, 3,648 of males and 3,409 of females, were registered, compared with 6,892 in 1905, 6,917 in 1904, and 6,772 in 1903. In Table III. is shown the number of births, males and females, the birth-rate, and the rate of natural increase for each, sub-district, and for the whole Borough. TABLE III. Births. Rates. Sub-District. Males. Females. Total. Birth-Rate. Rate of natural increase. Clapham 712 667 1,379 20.50 9.19 Putney 256 249 505 17.12 6.44 Streatham 897 853 1,750 18.47 8.88 Tooting 512 502 1,014 35.44 21.95 Wandsworth 1,271 1,138 2,409 26.57 14.49 Whole Borough 3,648 3,409 7,057 22.74 11.59 In all the sub-districts the birth-rate was lower than in the previous year. The lowest birth-rate was in Putney, where it was only 17.12 per 1,000; and the highest in Tooting, viz.:— 35.44 per 1,000. The actual number of births registered was lower in Clapham, Putney, and Streatham, and higher in Tooting and Wands- 9 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. worth, compared with the previous year, and the total increase over the whole Borough in the number of births was only 165, although the estimated increase of the population was 24,400. The following Table shows the birth-rate in the Borough, in the five sub-districts, in London, and in England and Wales, for the year, as well as the rates for the previous 10 years, compared with the decennial average. England and Wales. 1896 24.25 22.57 23.17 24.96 29.94 25.54 30.2 29.6 1897 24.19 23.82 22.85 24.01 30.10 25.56 30.1 29.5 1898 22.52 25.38 22.08 24.11 29.12 24.78 29.7 29.3 1899 25.45 23.79 22.75 29.60 29.22 25.83 29.7 29.1 1900 25.22 23.42 22.48 32.77 28.46 25.65 29.1 28.7 1901 25.33 22.10 22.42 34.09 29.69 26.02 29.0 28.5 1902 24.63 22.24 22.68 32.04 30.90 25.78 28.5 28.6 I903 26.87 22.22 21.47 33.19 30.16 26.22 28.4 28.4 1904 27.25 20.98 21.13 27.39 30.64 25.69 27.8 27.9 1905 22.09 18.73 19.95 35.52 26.76 23.64 27.0 27.2 Average for 10 yrs. 24.58 22.52 22.09 29.76 29.49 25.47 28.95 28.68 1906 20.50 17.12 18.47 35.44 26.57 22.74 26.6 27.0 The birth-rate in Table I. of the Local Government Board's Tables is calculated from the uncorrected population and is lower than the rate calculated from the corrected population. The birth-rate in each of the sub-districts, with the exception of Tooting, is lower than the decennial average, the greatest 10 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. reduction having occurred in Putney where the rate is 5.04 under this average. In Tooting, however, the rate is 5.68 above this average, while in the whole Borough the rate is 2.73 under it. The rate for the Borough is the lowest recorded, being .90 per 1,000 under the rate for last year. The birth-rate for London was 26.6 per 1,000, compared with 27.02 in 1905, 27.81 in 1904, and 28.95 the decennial average, while for England and Wales the rate was 27.0 per 1,000, compared with 27.2 in 1905, 27.9 in 1904, and 28.68 the decennial average. These rates show that the birth-rate has decreased more rapidly in this Borough than in London as a whole. There is, of course, one fallacy which must be mentioned, viz.:—that the rates for this Borough are calculated on an estimated population higher than the actual. The rate of natural increase, or the excess of births over deaths, was 11.59 per 1,000, compared with 12.50 in 1905, and 13.28 in 1904. This rate was lowest in Putney, viz.:—6.44, and highest in Tooting, viz.:—21.95. As stated in the Report for 1905, the diminution in the birthrate is real, and may be shown by comparing the births per 1,000 married females at ages 15-45. In 1901 this rate was 21.5 per cent.; in 1904, 21.0; in 1905, 19.4; and in 1906 it was only 18.3. In London as a whole this percentage was in 1901-3, 23.4; in 1904, 22.8; and in 1905, 21.9. The Registrar General in his report for London for 1905 with regard to the decline in the birth-rate, says: "There are sufficient grounds for stating that approximately about .21 per cent. of the birth-rate (calculated on the female population 15-45 years) in London during the past 35 years, is due to the decrease in the proportion of married women in the female population of conceptive ages; about .6 per cent. is due to the decrease of illegitimacy; Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 11 while the remaining 73 per cent. must be ascribed to the decreased fertility of married women, which is due in part to changes in their age constitution." 167 births occurred in the Workhouse, 12 of these belonging to Clapham, eight to Putney, 17 to Streatham, 12 to Tooting, and 47 to Wandsworth, 57 to Battersea, and 14 to other places. Of these 167 births, 108 were illegitimate, and of these seven belonged to Clapham, four to Putney, 15 to Streatham, nine to Tooting, 32 to Wandsworth, 30 to Battersea, and 11 to other places. In the Borough, with the exception of the Workhouse, 136 illegitimate births were registered, 35 in Clapham, four in Putney, 33 in Streatham, 29 in Tooting, and 35 in Wandsworth. The total number of illegitimate births registered in the Borough was 203, 42 of which belonged to Clapham, eight to Putney, 48 to Streatham, 38 to Tooting, and 67 to Wandsworth, compared with 165 in 1904. The percentage of illegitimate births to total births was 2.87, compared with 2.4 in 1905, and 3.7 for the whole of London for the year 1905. In the separate sub-districts this rate was, in Clapham 3.0, in Putney, 1.6, in Streatham 2.8, in Tooting 3.7, and in Wandsworth 2.8. Deaths. During the 52 weeks ending the 29th December, 1906, 3,341 deaths, 1,604 of males and 1,737 of females, were registered in the Borough, compared with 3,241 in 1905, 3,296 in 1904, and 3,007 in 1903. The total number of deaths is 100 more than occurred in 1905, but is 738 less than the corrected decennial average. The uncorrected death-rate for the whole Borough was 10.57 per 1,000, compared with 11.12 in 1905, 12.22 in 1904, and 11.69 12 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. in 1903. The average for the decennium 1896-1905 was 12.97, so that the rate for the year is 2.40 lower than the average and is the lowest ever recorded. In the separate sub-districts this rate varied very much, and it was especially high in Tooting owing to the number of deaths occurring in Institutions in the sub-district. The rate was 9.21 per 1,000 in Clapham, 8.89 in Putney, 8.01 in Streatham, 23.61 in Tooting, and 9.78 in Wandsworth. To ascertain the corrected death-rate corrections must be made to the above by the addition of the deaths in Outlying Institutions of persons belonging to the Borough, and the subtraction of all deaths in Public Institutions in the Borough of persons not belonging thereto, whose domiciles were in other parts of London. The total number of deaths, corrected for both internal and external Institutions, was 3,461, compared with 3,320 in 1905, 3,417 in 1904, 3,023 in 1903, 3,328 in 1902, 3,130 in 1901, and 4,158 the decennial average corrected for increase of population. The total number of deaths was thus 697 under this average, compared with 562 in 1905, 110 in 1904, and 433 in 1903. During the year 707 deaths occurred in Public Institutions and 64 in other places not Institutions in the Borough. Of these 771, 611 deaths were of persons not belonging and 160 of persons belonging to the Borough. The number of deaths in Public Institutions, etc., viz.:—771, is an increase of 42 compared with the previous year. There was, however, only an increase of six in Institutions, but an increase of 36 in other places. In the Hostel of God the number of deaths was 15 more, in St. Anne's Home seven less, in the Middlesex Lunatic Asylum 16 less, in the Metropolitan Asylums Board's Hospitals 15 more, in the Tooting Home 23 less, in the Prison one more, in the Workhouse one less, in the Royal Hospital for Incurables two less, in the Tooting Bee Asylum 22 more, in the Priory two more, and in other places 36 more than in the year 1905. In the Table it will be seen that in the deaths at other places, 11 occurred in Clapham, 16 in Putney, 17 in Streatham, two in Tooting, and 18 in Wandsworth. Table VI. shows the deaths in Public Institutions, etc., in the Borough of persons not belonging thereto, arranged as to ages, causes of death, and Institutions in which the deaths occurred. As in previous years the greatest number of deaths was due to Old Age, 154 of these having been registered, 60 of males and 94 of females, and of these 116 occurred in the Tooting Bee Asylum. In the Middlesex County Asylum the greatest number of deaths occurred from Tubercular disease of the Lungs, Old Age, and other causes, which include all diseases of the nervous system. In the Tooting Bee Asylum the greatest number of deaths occurred from Tubercular disease of the Lungs, Old Age, Softening of the Brain, Lobar Pneumonia, and all other causes. In the Hostel of God from Tubercular disease of the Lungs and Cancer; and in the Tooting Home from Tubercular disease of the Lungs and Old Age. In the Fever Hospitals one death occurred from Measles, 46 from Scarlet Fever, compared with 50; one from Whooping Cough; 30 from Diphtheria, compared with 20; seven from Enteric Fever, compared with 11; and nine from other causes, compared with 13 in 1905. 16 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Compared with 1905 there were 24 more deaths from Tubercular disease of the Lungs, 20 more from Old Age, and 10 more from Diphtheria; while there were four less from Scarlet Fever, 11 less from Cancer, and 32 less from all other causes. Of the 44 deaths in other places, nine were from Accidental and seven from Suicidal causes. Table VII. shows the number of deaths in Public Institutions in the Borough of persons belonging thereto, arranged according to cause of death, sex, age, and Institution in which the death occurred. The total number of deaths was 160, 84 of males and 74 of females, compared with 119, 66 of males and 53 of females, in 1905. 55 of the deaths occurred in the Fever Hospitals, compared with 50 in 1905; 60 in the Tooting Home, compared with 51; 10 in the Hostel of God, compared with one; six in the Workhouse, compared with five; nine in the Tooting Bee Asylum, compared with five; and 20 in other places, compared with six. If a comparison is to be made with other places it is necessary to again correct the corrected rate, viz. :—11.15, for age and sex distribution. This is done by multiplying the rate by a factor obtained by dividing the standard death-rate in England and Wales, at the Census of 1901, with the standard death-rate for the Borough at the same period. This factor has been ascertained to be 1.0547, which gives a rate for this Borough for the year 1906 of 11.76 per 1,000, compared with 12.24 in 1905, 13.60 in 1904, 12.87 in 1903, and 14.oo in 1902.
