In the separate sub-districts of the Borough, the death-rate was highest in Tooting—13.49 per 1,000, and lowest in Streatham—9.59 per 1,000. In 1905 Wandsworth had the highest rate, viz. :—13'oo, and Streatham the lowest, viz. :—1o.o6 per 1,000. The rate in Clapham was higher by .63 per 1,000 compared with 1905, lower in Putney by .02, lower in Streatham by '47, higher in Tooting by 1 .58, and lower in Wandsworth by .92 per 1,000. The rates in this Table do not agree with the rates in Table XI., as the rates in the latter Table are calculated on the total population without deducting the population of non-parishioners in Public Institutions in the Borough. The first Chart shows the annual death-rate per 1,000 of population during each week for the whole of London, and for each fortnight for the Borough. As was the case in 1905, the death-rate in this Borough was always lower than that for all London, the only time when the rates almost approximated being in the third week in September. This was due, as in previous years, to the increase in the number of deaths in this Borough from Diarrhoea and Zymotic Enteritis. The approximation would not be so apparent if the weekly rates for London and the Borough were compared. The next Chart shows the death-rate in this Borough for each fortnight, compared with the rates in the year 1905. This shows that in January the rate was lower in the first fortnight and higher in the second, lower in February, higher in the first fortnight in March, lower in the second and higher in the third, higher in April and May, higher in the first fortnight in June and lower in the second, lower in the first fortnight in July and higher in the second, lower in August, lower in the first fortnight in September and higher in the other two fortnights, and lower in October, November, and December. Only once during the year was the rate above 15 per 1,000, while in 1905 this rate was exceeded on three occasions, and on six occasions the rate was under 10 per 1,000. The lowest rate was recorded in the first fortnight in November when it was only 25 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. eight, and the highest in the second fortnight in September when it was 16.7 per 1,000. The high rate in the third fortnight in March was due to Influenza and diseases of the lungs, and the high rate in September to deaths from Diarrhoea, Zymotic Enteritis, and Enteritis, especially among children. The four Tables which follow are inserted by order of the Local Government Board. Table I. shows the births, birth-rate, deaths, and death-rate per 1,000 for the whole Borough for the year, and the ten preceding years; the total deaths of children under one year of age is also given, and the rate per 1,000 births registered. Table II. shows the population, the number of births and deaths registered, and the number of deaths under one year of age, for the whole Borough, as well as for the various subdistricts. It also shows these for each year since 1896, and is to a large extent a complete history of the mortality of the Borough for the last ten years. Table III. shows the number of cases of infectious disease notified during the year at all ages and at certain age periods in the Borough and in the several sub-districts, and also shows the number of cases removed from each sub-district to Hospital. Table IV. is the General Mortality Table for the whole Borough, showing the number of deaths at all ages, arranged as to cause of death. This also shows the total number of deaths in each sub-district, and the number of deaths in Public Institutions in the district, both of residents and non-residents. 26 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Note. - The deaths to be included in Column 7 of this table are the whole of those registered during the year as having actually occurred within the district or division. By the term " Non-residents " is meant persons brought into the district on account of sickness or infirmity, and dying in public institutions there; and by the term "Residents" is meant persons who have been taken out of the district on account of sickness or infirmity and have died in public institutions elsewhere. The 44 Public institutions" to be taken into account for the purposes of these tables are those into which persons are habitually received on account of sickness or infirmity, such as hospitals, workhouses and lunatic asylumns. Ages at Death. The following Table shows the percentage of deaths to total deaths at six age periods for each sub-district and for the whole Borough :— TABLE XVI. Sub-Districts. Under 1 year. 1 to 5 years. 5 to 15 years. 15 to 25 years. 25 to 65 years. 65 years and upwards Clapham 20.50 7.67 4.10 3.17 35.32 29.23 Putney 20.95 7.62 2.54 2.85 39.04 27.00 Streatham 22.11 7.59 1.76 3.30 31.91 33.33 Tooting 38.86 12.96 4.14 3.63 25.39 15.02 Wandsworth 27.58 9.40 3.56 3.74 34.53 21.19 Whole Borough 25.25 8.76 3.18 3.41 33.40 26.00 Compared with 1905 the percentage of deaths under one year to total deaths was .62 higher, this being due to the increased number of deaths of infants from diseases of the Digestive Organs; from one to five years 1.90 lower; from five to 15 years .61 lower; from 15 to 25 years .28 lower; from 25 to 65 years .5 higher; and from 65 years and upwards 1.66 higher. The increase in 1906 compared with 1905 is thus seen to have been at the extremes of life. As has been the case in former years, there is a considerable difference between the percentages in the sub-districts. Under one year Clapham had a percentage of only 20.5 and Putney of 20.95, while Wandsworth had a percentage of 27.58 and Tooting of 38.86; while at 65 years and upwards the percentage in Streatham was 33.33, and in Tooting only 15.02. These differences in rates are due to the difference in age distribution and social condition in the sub-districts, Tooting and Wandsworth containing a much larger proportion of persons under five years of age to total population, and also a larger proportion of the working classes. 32 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Infantile Mortality. The total number of deaths under one year was 874, compared with 818 in 1905, 851 in 1904, 743 in 1903, and 809 in 1902. Of these, 155 occurred in Clapham, 66 in Putney, 201 in Streatham, 150 in Tooting, and 302 in Wandsworth. The infantile mortality was 125 per 1,000 births, compared with 118 in 1905, 123 in 1904, 110 in 1903, and 126 in 1902. In the sub-districts this rate was 111 in Clapham, compared with 113 in 1905, and 122 the decennial average; 128 in Putney, compared with 109 and 129; 113 in Streatham, compared with 99 and 116; 146 in Tooting, compared with 148 and 147; and 131 in Wandsworth, compared with 128 and 140. Although the rate in 1906 was higher than in the three previous years, yet it was six per 1,000 under the decennial average in the Borough, and in each sub-district it was also lower than the decennial average. Table XIX. shows the deaths in the whole Borough, and the six following Tables the deaths in each registration sub-district. From notifiable diseases five deaths occurred, two from Scarlet Fever, and three from Diphtheria; 16 from Measles, 28 from Whooping Cough, 269 from Diarrhæal diseases, 125 from Premature Birth, 173 from Congenital defects or diseases, 42 34 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. from diseases of a Tuberculous nature, 112 from Respiratory diseases, 17 from non-Tuberculous Meningitis, and 85 from all other causes. Compared with 1905 there is an increase in the number of deaths of infants from Diarrhæal diseases, Tuberculous diseases, non-Tuberculous Meningitis, and Convulsions, and a decrease in the number of deaths from infectious diseases, wasting diseases, Syphilis, diseases of the Respiratory Organs, and other causes. In all the deaths with the exception of eight, three in Clapham, one in Streatham, two in Tooting, and two in Southfield, the cause of death was certified by a medical man or by the coroner after inquest. 32.03 per cent, of the deaths occurred in the first month, compared with 34.2 per cent, in 1905; 51.7 per cent, in the first three months, compared with 52.4; and 72.6 in the first six months, compared with 71.8 per cent. The Table also shows that 298, or 33.3 per cent, of the total deaths occurred from wasting diseases, and that 214, or 71.8 per cent., occurred under one month. Of the total deaths under one month, viz. :—286, 214 or 74.8 per cent, occurred from wasting diseases. From Diarrhceal diseases only 20 deaths occurred under one month out of a total of 269. The greatest mortality from this group occurred from the second to the eighth month. 42 deaths occurred from Tuberculous diseases, compared with 34 in 1905, and from diseases of the Respiratory Organs 112, compared with 136. The increase in the number of deaths from Meningitis compared with the previous year raised a suspicion that some of these cases have been cases of Cerebro-Spinal Meningitis, but enquiry into the symptoms of some of the cases did not verify this. At no time during the year have my services been required by any medical practitioner, although a circular was sent to every practising medical man in the Borough stating that I would see any suspicious case. At the same time a copy of the circular issued by the Local Government Board was also sent. Report 0f the Medical Officer of Health. 35 TABLE XIX. (Table 5.) Borough of Wandsworth. The Table which follows shows the infantile mortality rate per 1,000 births for the whole Borough, and for each sub-district, from certain causes and from all causes. In this Table the corrected number of births is used for the rates, the births occurring in the Workhouse being allocated to their respective sub-districts, and the births belonging to other places subtracted. In Clapham the mortality was very low from all causes, although from Developmental diseases it had the highest mortality. The mortality from Premature Birth was highest in Tooting, and this was also the case with regard to Diarrhœa. From Respiratory diseases the mortality was highest in Wandsworth and lowest in Streatham, from Tuberculous diseases highest in Tooting and lowest in Streatham, and from other causes highest in Tooting and lowest in Streatham. From all causes the infantile mortality was lowest in Clapham and highest in Tooting, and compared with 1905 this mortality was lower in Clapham and Tooting, but higher in Putney, Streatham, and Wandsworth. 43 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Compared with 1905, there has been an increase in the number of deaths from Diphtheria, Enteric Fever, Puerperal Fever, Influenza, Diarrhœa, and other Septic diseases, and a decrease in the number of deaths from Scarlet Fever, Erysipelas, Measles, and Whooping Cough. 45 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Compared with the corrected decennial average there has been an increase in the number of deaths from Puerperal Fever, Diarrhoea, and other Septic diseases, and a decrease in the number from Small-pox, Scarlet Fever, Diphtheria, Enteric Fever, Measles, Whooping Cough and Influenza. The greatest decrease occurred from Diphtheria, Measles, and Whooping Cough, and the greatest increase from Diarrhoea. From all Zymotic diseases the death-rate was 1.70 (1.54 in Clapham, 1.49 in Putney, 1.31 in Streatham, 3.04 in Tooting, and 1.89 in Wandsworth), compared with 1.57 in 1905, 1.93 in 1904, 1.72 in 1903, and 2.19 the decennial average. The rate from the principal Zymotic diseases, viz.:—Smallpox, Measles, Scarlet Fever, Whooping Cough, Diphtheria, Enteric Fever, and Diarrhoea, was 1.36 per 1,000 (1.18 in Clapham, 1.01 in Putney, .95 in Streatham, 2.72 in Tooting, and 1.59 in Wandsworth), compared with 1.30 in 1905, 1.6o in 1904, 1.49 in 1903, and 1.68 in 1902. The rate in London was 1.94 per 1,000, compared with r68 in 1905, 2.14 in 1904, 1.73 in 1903, and 2.21 in 1902. The rate for England and Wales was 1.73, in the 76 Great Towns, 2.24, in the 142 Smaller Towns 1.71, and in the Rural England and Wales 1.18. In out-lying Institutions 52 deaths occurred from Zymotic diseases (17 belonging to Clapham, five to Putney, four to Streatham, six to Tooting, and 20 to Wandsworth), compared with 67 in 1905, 58 in 1904, and 32 in 1903. Of these deaths, 24 occurred in the Union Infirmary, four from Measles, three from Influenza, six from Whooping Cough, one from Enteric Fever, one from Zymotic Enteritis, five from Erysipelas, three from Puerperal Fever, and one from other Septic diseases; 16 in General and Special Hospitals, one from Measles, one from Diphtheria, 10 from Diarrhoea and Zymotic Enteritis, one from Puerperal Fever, and three from other Septic diseases; and 12 in the Hospitals of the Metropolitan Asylums 46 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Board, three from Scarlet Fever, eight from Diphtheria, and one from Enteric Fever. In internal Institutions 90 deaths occurred of persons not belonging to the Borough, compared with 89 in 1905, and 67 in 1904. Of these 85 occurred in the Hospitals of the Metropolitan Asylums Board at Tooting, 46 from Scarlet Fever, 30 from Diphtheria, seven from Enteric Fever, one from Measles, and one from Whooping Cough. Two deaths from Erysipelas occurred in the Middlesex County Asylum, one from Influenza in the Royal Hospital for Incurables, and two in other places. Of the total deaths from Zymotic diseases, 50 occurred in the Fever Hospitals at Tooting, 13 from Scarlet Fever, 31 from Diphtheria, and six from Enteric Fever, and one death from Erysipelas and one from other Septic diseases in the Tooting Home. Compared with the decennial average corrected for increase of population there has been a decrease in all the notifiable diseases with the exception of Puerperal Fever, where there has been an increase of 9.9. During the year 1905, of 6,893 children whose births were registered, 5,145 or 74.6 per cent. were successfully vaccinated, compared with 73.5 per cent. in 1904, 73.7 per cent. in 1903, and 72.4 per cent. in 1902. This is the highest percentage that has been recorded since 1894, when it was 75.8. The next Table shows the percentage of children vaccinated to total births registered in the Borough and for the vaccination sub-district for the year 1905, as well as for the 10 years preceding; the average for the 10 years is also shown. The increase in the Borough of successfully vaccinated, compared with the decennial average is 4.9. In Clapham the increase was 7.7, in Putney 14.6, in Streatham 4.4, and in Wandsworth 3.0. Compared with 1904 there has been a very decided improvement in the number of cases of successful vaccinations in Clapham, but, as was the case in that year also, the number of children remaining on the books as unvaccinated was highest in Clapham, being 170, compared with none in Putney, 18 in Streatham, and 25 in Wandsworth. Putney shows an improvement compared with 1904, as also does Wandsworth and Streatham, but in the latter sub-district the improvement is only .5 per cent. In the Local Government Board's returns for the year 1903, of the children whose births were registered in England and Wales, 75.3 per cent. are recorded as successfully vaccinated, 9.7 per cent. as having died unvaccinated, while 10.7 per cent. were not finally accounted for as regards vaccination at the time the returns were made. In London the cases not finally accounted for amounted to 20.7 per cent., compared with 21.3 per cent. in the previous year. This high percentage is due largely to default in several Unions in the East End. 580 infants died unvaccinated, a percentage of 8 4, compared with 8.6 in 1904, and 8.3 in 1903. 53 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Of the remaining 1,168 infants, 19 or .27 per cent. were stated to have been insusceptible to vaccination, 140 or 2'o per cent, in respect to whom certificates of conscientious objection were received, 265 or 3.8 per cent. had the vaccination postponed by medical certificate, leaving 531 or 77 per cent. as removed, not to be traced, or otherwise unaccounted for, while 213 or 3.1 per cent. remained on the books as unvaccinated, compared with 5.4 remaining as unvaccinated in 1904. If the number of deaths of infants who died unvaccinated be deducted from the total births, then of the 6,313 remaining infants 81.5 per cent. were successfully vaccinated, compared with 80.5 in 1904, and 80.4 in 1903; .3 were insusceptible of vaccination, compared with .48 in 1904, and .5 in 1903; 4.2 per cent. were under medical certificate of postponement, compared with 3.4 in 1904, and 3 in 1903; and 2.2 per cent. in regard to whom certificates of conscientious objection had been obtained, compared with 2.1 in 1904, and 1.6 in 1903; leaving 11.8 per cent. unaccounted for as regards vaccination, compared with 13.1 in 1904, and 14.3 in 1903. Scarlet Fever. 1,069 cases of Scarlet Fever were notified during the year, compared with 1,494 in 1905, 744 in 1904, and 822 in 1903. 180 of these were in Clapham, 34 in Putney, 255 in Streatham, 245 in Tooting, and 355 in Wandsworth. 895 or 83.8 per cent. of the cases were removed to hospital where 16 died, and 174 or i6.2 per cent. were treated at home with three deaths. The total number of deaths was 19, of which six were in Clapham, none in Putney, three in Streatham, three in Tooting, and seven in Wandsworth. The percentage of cases removed to Hospital shows a slight decrease compared with 1905, the percentage in that year being 84.1, but it is an increase on previous years, as in 1904 the percentage was 81.6; in 1903, 78.1; in 1902, 75; and in 1901, 70.6. The case mortality during the year was 178 per cent., compared with 1.87 in 1905, 1.74 in 1904, and 2.19 in 1903. The mortality among the cases sent to the Fever Hospitals was 1.79 per cent., compared with 2.14 in 1905, and among those treated at home 172 per cent., compared with .42 in 1905. The mortality in both these groups did not differ much in 1906, while in 1905 the percentage of deaths in hospital-treated cases was five times greater than among those treated at home. 55 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The proportion of cases notified per 1,000 of population was 3.4, compared with 5.1 in 1905, 2.8o in 1904, and 3.2 in 1903, and in the County of London 4.3, compared with 4.1, 2.89, and 2.7. The proportion of cases notified was thus .9 per 1,000 lower than that for the whole of London, while in 1905 it was one per 1,000 higher. Sub-Districts. 1903 1904 1905 1906 Clapham 2.4 1.8 4.1 2.7 Putney 3.8 3.0 1.8 1.1 Streatham 2.3 2.6 3.2 2.6 Tooting 3.0 3.1 7.0 7.9 Wandsworth 4.1 3.2 8.2 3.7 Whole Borough 3.2 2.8 5.1 3.4 The rate in Clapham, Putney, Streatham, and Wandsworth, is lower than in 1905, but in Tooting it is higher. In Clapham the disease was most prevalent in January, February and May, while it was at its minimum in September. There was nothing special in the incidence of this disease in Clapham during the year, with the exception of eight cases in August which were caused by an unrecognised case. In Putney the number of cases was considerably under the average, no cases having been notified in the months of February, July, August, and September. The maximum number of cases, viz:—eight, was notified in December, and 20 out of the 34 notified during the year occurred in October, November and December. No death occurred among these 34 cases. 56 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. In Streatham the number of cases was slightly under the number for 1905. The maximum number of cases was notified in February and the minimum in May. The increase in February was to some extent due to the occurrence of 12 cases in one house in Balham caused by an unrecognised case. Eight of these cases were returned from hospital as not having had Scarlet Fever after being kept under observation for several days. The only other outbreak of any consequence was in Streatham, where 12 cases were notified from a tenement house of nine rooms occupied by four families. These cases were notified in September, November, and December, but the first case in September was probably infected by a previous case next door notified at the end of August. The first symptoms appeared on the30th August, but it was not notified until the 3rd September, and in the meantime other seven cases had been infected. The second group of four cases were in all probability due to one of the first group of cases returned from hospital on the 10th November. All the cases were mild and no deaths occurred. There was great carelessness on the part of the occupiers of the house in exposing themselves and their children to infection, although repeatedly warned of the danger that was incurred by so doing and by not seeking medical advice earlier. In Tooting the rate was very high, being 7.9 or .9 above the rate in 1905. The cases in Tooting were not confined to any special locality, but occurred over the whole area of the subdistrict. The maximum number of cases, viz., 29, were notified in the month of June and the minimum in December, viz., 13. Deducting the 21 cases notified from the Fountain and Grove Fever Hospitals, the rate would be 7'8. One reason of the greater number of cases in Tooting is that the number of children at school age in that sub-district is greater compared with the total population than it is in any other part of the Borough, and it is among children at school age that Scarlet Fever most frequently occurs. 57 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. At the Census of 1901, the percentage of children from four to 13 years in the whole Borough was 18.9, while in Tooting this percentage is approximately 22.1. I have not been able to trace any connection between the Fever Hospitals and the increased prevalence of Scarlet Fever in this sub-district, as, with the exception of one street, the locality in the immediate neighbourhood of these hospitals did not suffer any more severely than any other part of the sub-district. This exception was due, not to the Fever Hospitals, but to two unrecognised cases which were allowed to mix with other children in the street before the disease was recognised. There was, however, a localised outbreak in another street due to unrecognised cases. In this, 12 cases occurred in six households, five in one house, three in another, and one each in four houses. The increased prevalence in Tooting did not lead to an increased mortality, as only two deaths occurred out of the 245 cases notified. In Wandsworth the percentage of cases notified to total population was only 3.7, compared with 8.2 in 1905. The disease was not nearly so prevalent in this sub-district as was the case in 1905. The maximum number of cases was notified in February and the minimum in March. There was a slight recrudescence of this disease in the Royal Patriotic Asylum in February, when 16 cases were notified, and in March, when seven cases were notified. In 1905, 51 cases were notified, chiefly in October, and in 1906, 24 cases, the last case having been notified on the 26th March. All these were removed to hospital, but no deaths resulted. The death-rate for the Borough was .07 under the decennial average. All the sub-districts show a decrease, with the exception of Tooting, which is the same as such average. The Chart shows the number of cases notified during each week of the year. The maximum number of cases was notified in the second week of February, when 24 cases occurred, and there was another maximum in the first week of November. The increase in February was general all over the district, but in the end of the year, during the months of October, November and December, while the disease was more prevalent, it was most so in Balham, Tooting and Wandsworth. The smallest number of notifications was received in the first two weeks of September, when only four cases occurred, two in each week. Only one special outbreak occurred during the year, viz.:— in Brandlehow Road School; but in one Institution in the Borough, viz.:—the Hambro Orphanage, Roehampton, considerable difficulty was experienced in getting the disease eradicated. Only seven cases were notified, but the cases occurred at irregular intervals, pointing, in my opinion, to the fact that unrecognised cases existed. The first case was notified on the 5th February, followed by another on the 27th March, then other cases occurred on the 29th March, 11th and 25th April, 4th May, and 12th June. On the 23rd May, 23 swabs were taken, but no Klebs Loeffler bacilli were found. In 14 Hoffman's pseudo Diphtheria bacilli were found to be present, while in 11 a negative result was obtained. On the 1st June, 14 swabs were taken, and in one the Klebs Loeffler bacillus was found, in seven the Hoffman's bacillus, and in six there was no result. On the nth June, 11 swabs were taken, and in one both the Klebs Loeffler and the Hoffman's bacilli were found, in three the Hoffman's bacillus was found, and in seven the result was negative. 64 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. On the 23rd June, the three in whom the Hoffman's bacillus was found were again swabbed, and in two the Hoffman's bacillus was still present, while in the other the result was negative. On the 3rd April I made personal inquiries at the Institution, and I came to the conclusion that the first case had been caused by a child who had been admitted at the end of January from the country, and that the other cases were due to secondary infection. I advised the medical attendant to swab the throats of all the inmates, but this was not done thoroughly until the 23rd May. The cases were all removed to hospital and the premises were thoroughly disinfected, the children having been sent away for a time, and after their return no further cases occurred. On the 1st October a case was notified to me from No. 66, St. Ann's Hill, Wandsworth, a home for boys of defective intellect belonging to the Metropolitan Asylums Board, and under the special circumstances I advised the medical attendant to at once swab the throats of the inmates. This was done on the 4th October, with the result that of 19 swabs taken, in one the Klebs Loeffler bacillus was found, in seven the Hoffman's bacillus, and in 11 no bacilli were found. On the 4th October a second case and on the 5th a third case were notified, the fourth case on the 8th, and the fifth case on the 9th. On the 8th, 16 swabs were taken, in one of which the Klebs Loeffler bacillus was found, in three the Hoffman's bacillus, and 12 were negative. 14 swabs were taken on the 24th October, but no Klebs Loeffler bacilli were found in any of the specimens, although in six the Hoffman's bacillus was present. The cases which showed the presence of the Hoffman's bacillus were treated and kept under observation for some time, but no further cases of true Diphtheria occurred. Of these 531 cases, 225 or 42.3 per cent. were not in attendance at any school, compared with 47 per cent. in 1905, while 306 or 57.7 per cent. were at school, compared with 52.6 in 1905. 48 cases occurred in connection with private schools, a considerable increase compared with last year, and 16 attended schools outside the Borough. With the exception of Brandlehow Road School, Wandsworth, where seven cases occurred in January, six in February and 18 during the year, there were no schools where exceptional increase was noted. The following special report on this outbreak was made on the 8th February:— "I have to report the occurrence of an outbreak of Diphtheria in connection with Brandlehow Road School, Wandsworth. 67 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. "The first case was notified to me on the 17th January, and was removed to hospital on the 18th. This child, B. H., residing in Fawe Park Road, Wandsworth, was a scholar in Room D, Infants' Department, Brandlehow Road School. The first symptoms of the disease appeared on the 15th January, and there had been no previous illness among the other members of this family. The usual disinfection was carried out by the staff and no further cases have occurred in this household. "The second case was notified on the 22nd, the patient, A. P., a boy aged 6, was also a scholar in Room D, Infants' Department. "I made enquiries and ascertained that these children attended the same department. I at once communicated with the Medical Officer of Health, Education Department, London County Council, and advised that the throats of the children in this department should be examined and swabs taken if necessary, as I was under the impression that an unrecognised case or cases was the cause of the outbreak. The Assistant to the Medical Officer, Education Department, visited the school on the 24th and took 48 cultures, and on the 25th he reported to me that two children gave a growth of Diphtheria bacilli and eight Hoffman's, or the pseudo Diphtheria bacillus. "All these children were excluded from school, and the Head Mistress was instructed by the Medical Officer to notify all cases of sore throat to me. "The school was ordered by the Medical Officer, Education Department, to be closed on the 24th till the 29th, but I informed him that it would be necessary to keep it closed for another week so that my enquiries could be completed, and that the disinfection might be efficiently performed. "The names and addresses of the 10 children reported on by the Assistant Medical Officer were noted and information was sent to the parents of these children advising them to call in their medical attendant. 68 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. "On the 25th another notification was received, the patient, a boy of four years, attending the same school. "The two cases in which the true bacillus had been isolated were notified by the Medical Officer, Education Department, and another case was notified on the 29th January. The total number of cases that have occurred in connection with this department was seven, but in addition five other cases occurred in the immediate neighbourhood, one of these attending another school, and other four relations of one of the children notified. "Cultures were taken from the throats of 10 patients in the neighbourhood, and in five, four of which were members of the same family, the bacillus associated with Diphtheria was found, in three Hoffman's pseudo Diphtheria bacillus was present, and in two the bacillus was not found. One of the cases where the Hoffman's bacillus was present had been examined by the Assistant Medical Officer, Education Department, and at that time the true Diphtheria bacillus was found. "An enquiry into the history of the dates of the first symptoms elicited the fact that a girl of seven years attending the school and residing in Fawe Park Road had had a sore throat about the 10th January and continued at school. The throat of this child was swabbed and the Diphtheria bacillus was found. "The two cases first notified proved fatal. "A review of the whole of the circumstances in connection with this outbreak only emphasizes the fact which has been previously noted, that carrier cases, or cases attending school with the Diphtheria bacillus present with very few clinical symptoms, invariably cause a serious outbreak of the disease, more especially if the carrier case or cases attend infants' departments of public schools. "One feature of this outbreak which has also been previously observed is the association of the true bacillus and Hoffman's bacillus. All the facts which have been observed 69 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. in this outbreak appear to point to a distinct relationship between these two bacilli, either that they are of the same species, or that the Hoffman bacillus renders the affected individual more liable to infection by the true bacillus. "Enquiries as to the condition of the school premises elicited the fact that the whole of the basement of the school had been flooded with sewage on the 24th November, 1904. This sewage was due to the accidental stopping of the main sewer in Brandlehow Road. The whole of the basement was cleared of sewage on the 28th November. It is quite possible that the presence of this sewage in the basement gave rise to a condition of the throats of the children in the Infants' department which would render them liable to infection by a specific germ, and it seems as if the children in this department had been infected by the Hoffman bacillus first, and secondly by the true Diphtheria bacillus. That sore throat would occur among children attending the Infants' department is what one would have anticipated, as this department is situated immediately above the cellar, and the smell from this cellar would easily permeate into the whole of the Infants' department. "The outbreak is now, in my opinion, quite at an end as far as the school is concerned. The whole of the Infants' department was thoroughly disinfected and cleansed." Of the total number of cases of Diphtheria, 182 or 32.5 per cent. occurred among children under five years of age, 304 or 54.2 per cent. from five to 15 years, and 75 or 13.3 per cent. over 15 years. Of the 439 cases removed to hospital, 30 were reported to be not suffering from Diphtheria. Drainage defects were found in 71 of the houses in which cases of Diphtheria occurred. The total number of deaths was 15, six in Clapham, two in Putney, two in Streatham, none in Tooting, and five in Wandsworth, compared with 11 in 1905, 11 in 1904, and 19 in 1903. The number of deaths, although four more than in 1905, was nine under the corrected decennial average. The proportion of cases notified per 1,000 of population was .30, compared with .33 in 1905, .27 in 1904, and .41 in 1903, and with .34 for the whole of London. The corrected death-rate from fever was '048 per 1,000, compared with .04 in 1905, .04 in 1904, and .07 in 1903; while the rate for the whole of London was .05, compared with .05 in 1905, .06 in 1904, and .08 in 1903. Chart showing the number of cases of Enteric Fever notified during each week of the year 1906. 73 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The death-rate in the separate sub-districts was as follows:— in Clapham .08, in Putney .06, in Streatham .02, and in Wandsworth .05 per 1,000. Of the 53 cases removed to hospital 16 were found not to be. suffering from Enteric Fever. The Chart shows the number of cases notified in each week during the year. Of the 96 cases notified, 16 were not Enteric Fever, and 13 cases occurred in H.M. Prison, where a special enquiry was made by Government officials, leaving 67 cases of Enteric, regarding which special enquiry was made as to the probable cause of infection. In 15 of the cases the infection was probably due to eating shell-fish, in seven to watercress, in nine the disease was contracted outside the Borough (one in Egypt, one in Austria, and seven in other places), while six were infected by previous cases. With regard to the cases probably infected by eating shellfish, six were from oysters, one from mussels, one from cockles and seven from winkles. Four of the cases from eating shell-fish, two from oysters and two from winkles, were fatal. The following are the particulars of the 15 cases probably due to shell-fish :— (1.) S. L., notified on the 19th March. Had oysters about a fortnight previous to first symptoms from a local dealer. (2.) H. B., notified on the 8th October. Had oysters at Ramsgate about the end of August, and the first symptoms appeared about the 14th September. This patient was a habitual eater of oysters and cockles, and besides partaking of oysters at Ramsgate had several lots of cockles from Southend. This case proved fatal. (3.) F. W., notified on the 2nd November. Had oysters at Bognor a fortnight previous to appearance of first symptems. This case also proved fatal. 74 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. (4.) K. A., notified on the 9th November. Had oysters on the 10th October purchased from local dealers, and the first symptoms appeared about the 27th October. (5.) A. H., notified on the 22nd November. Had oysters at a shop in town on several occasions before appearance of first symptoms. (6.) H. S., notified on the 26th November. Patient had oysters about the 1st November, and the first symptoms appeared on the 19th November. The oysters were purchased at a shop in the city. (7.) T. W., notified on the 8th September. Had cockles at Heme Bay on several occasions up to the 19th August there, and the first symptoms appeared on the 29th August. (8.) H. O., notified on the 12th October. Had winkles at Newquay where he was on holiday about a fortnight previous to the appearance of the first symptoms. The Medical Officer of Health of that district was communicated with, but he reported that he did not think that the case could have 0fen infected there as the drainage outfall is a considerable distance from the mussel-bearing beds, and he did not think that there were any shell-fish near the sewer outlet. The patient stated that he picked the winkles from the rocks, but only ate a few as he noticed that they had a peculiar taste. (9-15.) These seven cases were all caused by eating winkles, and were the subject of a special report. ENTERIC FEVER AND SHELL-FISH. For some years I have, in my Annual Reports, called attention to cases of Enteric Fever caused by the eating of shell-fish. In any case where the vendor of the shell-fish could be found, the name of the wholesale dealer was ascertained, and this information was sent to the Medical Officer of Health for the City of London, in whose district Billingsgate is situated. In many of 75 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. the cases action was taken by the City Authorities and as a result the sale of polluted shell-fish from certain localities was stopped. This year 1 have again to report the occurrence of several cases of Enteric Fever caused by shell-fish, but in these cases the shell-fish was sent from a remote part of Scotland, and what is more serious from a public health point of view, from a part where polluted shell-fish was known to exist, as attention had been called to the locality by an outbreak of Enteric Fever in Glasgow in 1903, when the shell-fish were gathered on the same spot. In the Annual Report of the Medical Officer of Health for the County of Argyll for 1903, Dr. McNeill, the Medical Officer of Health, states that 27 cases of Enteric Fever had been notified between the 18th and the 24th August, all the patients having eaten shell-fish gathered on the shore of Loch Gilp, a small loch in Argyllshire, into which the sewage of the Burgh of Lochgilphead, the Argyll and Bute Asylum, the Poorhouse, and a number of private houses is discharged without treatment. Dr. McNeill states that at low tide the sea recedes about one mile from the head of the loch, and that the foreshore teems with shell-fish. Specimens of these shell-fish were taken and examined bacteriologically and it was found that those collected nearest the sewer showed the existence of a virulent bacillus of excremental origin (bacillus coli), and that in those gathered at a distance the bacillus was absent. It was also found that the bacillus typhosus, the bacillus of Typhoid Fever, was present in these surface cockles, and in one cockle it was obtained almost in pure culture. Dr. McNeill was of opinion that it was doubtful if the Local Authority could prohibit the gathering of shell-fish from polluted areas, but urged on Local Authorities the necessity of preventing the pollution of the foreshore where shell-fish are gathered with crude sewage. Although the number of cases in this Borough caused by eating shell-fish was few, there is little doubt that they were due to shell-fish collected in this area. 76 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The first case was notified on the 3rd February, one on the 2nd March, one on the 9th April, one on the 17th April, one on the 19th April, one on the 23rd April, and one on the 24th April, a total of seven, two of which proved fatal. Four of these occurred in the same house. It was not until the second case was notified that attention was directed to shell-fish as the cause of the disease as no information could be obtained in regard to the case notified on the 3rd February. The information was to the effect that the patients in every case partook of winkles bought from a local dealer who in turn bought them from a salesman in Billingsgate market. As soon as I was aware that the winkles had been bought at Billingsgate, I (on March 26th) communicated with the Medical Officer of Health for the City of London, who replied on the 3rd April to the effect that samples had been taken from the dealer and examined by Dr. Klein who reported that they did not show general pollution, nor did they contain the microbes searched for. On the 10th April I again wrote Dr. Collingridge informing him that another case had been notified and asking for information regarding the source of the cockles, to which he replied on the nth April that the winkles came from the West Coast of Scotland, and were collected from Oban, Skye, and Loch Fyne. As soon as I was aware that the winkles had come from Loch Fyne, or its neighbourhood, I communicated with Dr. McNeill, the Medical Officer of Health to the County of Argyll, who informed me that in August and September, 1903, an outbreak of Enteric Fever, due to the consumption of shell-fish, occurred in Glasgow, and that the shell-fish had been gathered in Loch Gilp Head. On the 8th May I received another letter from Dr. McNeill in which he stated that the polluted area had been under observation and that shell-fish from this area had been forwarded to several dealers at Billingsgate. Copies of the correspondence were sent to the Medical Officer of Health for the City of London and he reported the facts to his Sanitary Committee in May, 1906, and also that he was in 77 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. munication with the Medical Officer of Health for the County of Argyll with a view of ascertaining whether the water was sewage polluted at a number of other places in the neighbourhood of Loch Gilp Head, from which he was informed fishermen gather fish consigned to Billingsgate. This outbreak once more shows the necessity of further legislation being necessary for the purpose of giving powers to Local Authorities to inspect shefl-fish layings and to prohibit the sale of shell-fish from polluted layings or beds. In seven of the cases, one of which proved fatal, the patient had partaken of watercress previous to the appearance of the first symptoms, and in all the cases the watercress was purchased from itinerant dealers whose identity was difficult to establish. In six of the cases the infection was traced to a previous case in the same house; all of these cases recovered; while of the nine cases where the disease was contracted outside the Borough, one was fatal. In every case the drains of the houses were tested, but in only four was any result obtained, while in all the other cases the sanitary conditions were satisfactory. Cases of Mistaken Diagnosis. In 93 cases (47 of Scarlet Fever, 30 of Diphtheria, and 16 of Enteric Fever), information was received from the Metropolitan Asylums Board that the patients were not suffering from the disease notified or any other notifiable disease, compared with 97 in 1905, 94 in 1904, and 106 in 1903. Of the 1,386 cases of these diseases removed to hospital the diagnosis in 93, or 6.7 per cent., was incorrect, compared with 5.2 per cent. in 1905, 10 in 1904, and 9.5 in 1903. Enteric Fever is responsible for the highest percentage, viz., 30.1, and Scarlet Fever the lowest, viz , 5.2. 78 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. There has been a very satisfactory decrease in the percentage of cases of mistaken diagnosis in Diphtheria, this having fallen from i8'i in 1904 and 11.9 in 1905 to 68 in 1906. This is no doubt due to the greater care taken by medical practitioners in diagnosis by having a bacteriological examination made before certifying that the patient is suffering from this disease. The case-rate per 1,000 births was 3"5, while for the whole of London this rate was 2'4- During the year a copy of the following Regulations, which had been drawn up by the Society of Medical Officers of Health, was handed to every nurse who had been in attendance on a case of Puerperal Fever, and in all the cases the necessary disinfection was carried out. Seven nurses had free baths at the Disinfecting Station, Tooting. After disinfection and cleansing the appended certificate isgiven to the nurse. REGULATIONS for the Disinfection of Midwives after Attendance upon Patients Suffering from Puerperal Fever. Personal Disinfection. The midwife, after attending a septic case, should take a complete bath, including the thorough washing of the hair. An order for a free bath can be obtained from any of the Sanitary Inspectors or on application at the Public Health Department, 90, East Hill, Wandsworth. Disinfection of hands.—The careful disinfection of the hands requires strict attention. The nails should be trimmed. The hands should be washed with soap and water, rinsed in clean water, soaked in methylated spirit, and finally soaked in a solution of corrosive sublimate (1 in 1,000) or other equally efficient disinfectant, for about 10 minutes. 81 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Disinfection of Clothing. All articles of clothing worn while attending a septic case should be changed for entirely fresh ones, the used clothing being handed over to the Sanitary Authority for disinfection. Disinfection of Apparatus. The bag and apparatus used by the midwife should be handed over to the Sanitary Authority for disinfection. It is considered advisable that gum elastic catheters and syringes should be destroyed. Metal and other articles which will not be injured by the process should be boiled. The lining of the bag should be removed and destroyed, while the bag itself should be washed with corrosive sublimate (1 in 1,000) or other equally efficient disinfectant. Form of Certificate. I hereby Certify that................., who has recently been in attendance upon a patient suffering from "Puerperal Fever," has undergone personal cleansing and disinfection, and that her clothing, instruments, and other apparatus have been disinfected by the Sanitary Authority to my satisfaction. P. CALDWELL SMITH, M.D., Medical Officer of Health. Epidemic Influenza. 70 deaths were caused by Influenza, 34 of males and 36 of females, an increase of 29 compared with 1905, but a decrease of 16.5 compared with the corrected decennial average. 14 of the deaths occurred in Clapham, nine in Putney, 27 in Streatham, five in Tooting, and 15 in Wandsworth. From enquiries made among a number of medical men practising in the Borough I have ascertained that a considerable proportion of cases of Influenza occurring in the Borough in the latter part of the year were of a different type to those of previous years as gastro-enteric symptoms were prominent. The greatest number of deaths, however, occurred from diseases of the respiratory organs subsequent to Influenza, only one death having been registered as being due to abdominal Influenza. The increased mortality during the year from Diarrhoea was largely due to climatic conditions, the temperature having been greatly in excess of the average, and the rainfall low. These climatic conditions, combined with carelessness and ignorance of the proper method of feeding, were responsible for the majority of the deaths of these infants. Owing to the increased number of deaths in the third quarter of the year, it was deemed advisable to submit the following special report to the Council. REPORT of the Medical Officer of Health on the Mortality from Diarrhœa during the Third Quarter of 1906. In the third quarter of the year ending the 29th September, 188 deaths occurred from Diarrhoea and Zymotic Enteritis, and 46 from simple Enteritis, a total of 234. The mortality from Diarrhoea was 2.50 per 1,000 for the whole Borough, 1.53 in Clapham, 1.92 in Putney, 1.27 in Streatham, 7 08 in Tooting, and 3.20 in Wandsworth. The mortality was thus highest in Tooting and Wandsworth. The mortality, although above the average, compares favourably with other large towns, the figures for which are available. For examples the rates in the following towns were as follows, viz.:— London 1.81 Huddersfield 2.24 Croydon 3.07 Leeds 4.45 St. Helens 4.47 Liverpool 5.45 The rate per 1,000 births was as follows in the different wards:— Clapham North 75 Clapham South 51 Putney 126 Streatham 104 Balham 75 Tooting 173 Southfield 136 Fairfield 51 Springfield 101 Whole Borough 100 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 91 In the Borough of Battersea this rate was 92, and in London as a whole 112. Of the total deaths from Diarrhoea and Enteritis, 180 were of children under one year, and special enquiry was made in each death to ascertain how the child was fed. The total number of deaths was 180, and in only six of these could no information be obtained, chiefly on account of the fact that the parents had removed from the district. In 11, or 6.1 per cent. of the deaths, the infant was nursed by the mother, whereas 91.1 per cent. were otherwise fed, the majority on cows' milk or condensed milk. 20 of the deaths were of illegitimate children and eight were nurse children who had been born in other parts of London. 48 of the infants were either prematurely born or were delicate from birth, and under normal circumstances these would probably have succumbed in the first two months of life. As regards social status of the parents (deducting the 20 illegitimate deaths), it was found that 61 were labourers, 36 artizans, 33 small tradesmen, while the other 30 were of various occupations. It is hardly possible to state exactly from these statistics whether the work which has been carried out by the Inspectors of the Council has been the means of preventing many deaths from this disease, although it may be shown by reference to the figures that such work has been beneficial. For example, in Tooting where the mortality was greatest, of the 45 deaths 17 had been previously visited by the Inspector after notification of the birth. The mortality among those visited previously was only 3.6, while among those not visited this mortality was 8'3 per cent. In Wandsworth, however, the figures do not show such satisfactory results, the mortality among those visited in Springfield Ward having been 3.9, and among those not visited 3.5 per cent. 92 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. It must be noted, however, that the visits of the Inspector are only made among the labouring classes, and that of the 397 births not visited in the Springfield Ward the great majority were among persons of a higher social condition, among whom the mortality from Diarrhoea is very small. From a careful examination of these returns I am decidedly of opinion that the work of the Inspectors has been of the very greatest benefit, and taking into consideration the great increase of population, especially of the working-class population, the mortality is lower than was expected. The meteorological conditions were this year most favourable for all intestinal diseases, and over the whole of England the mortality from these diseases has been above the average. As will be seen from the Table the Inspectors chiefly confine their work to the Tooting, Fairfield, and Springfield Wards, and I am of opinion that it would be most desirable for the Council to appoint one Health Visitor, who could, under the Sanitary Inspectors, undertake the visiting of houses after notification of births and who could devote all her time to such work. If the appointment were made it would then be possible to extend the work to the other Wards of the Borough. 93 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. During the year the female Sanitary Inspectors have continued the work begun in 1905, in visiting homes where births occurred, and the following report gives the result of such visits and a general account of the work carried out:— "We have the honour of submitting to you our report on the work we have done amongst infants during the past year. "The visiting has been confined to the Parish of Tooting, the poorer parts of Balham, and the Fairfield and Springfield Wards of Wandsworth. Even in this circumscribed area it has been impossible to visit as frequently as would be desirable owing to the limited time at our disposal for this work. As under present circumstances only half the number of mothers can be visited a second time, it will be readily conceded that the results cannot be so satisfactory as if more frequent visits and help were given. We are glad to say that our advice has been in many instances gratefully received, and now that the work is more widely known the visits are looked forward to. "Reference to Table XLV. will show the total number of visits paid after notification of birth, the method of feeding at the first visit, and the number of infants visited who died during the year. The ineffectual visits mentioned are largely due to the shifting population in the poorer districts. Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 95 In many instances the family had removed, in some cases wrong addresses had been given, and frequently we had to call two, three or even as many as six times before finding the mother at home. "Table XLVI. shows the number of infants visited a second time and the method of feeding then found, the average age at the second visit being from four to five months. "It will be seen from Table XLV. that out of the 1,626 infants visited 50 were having food in addition to the breast. "The excuse invariable was that the child 'was not satisfied' with the mother's milk, and so bread, baked flour, or, in the majority of instances, nursery biscuits, were added. At the second visit we found that 36 out of the 50 had on our advice discontinued the food; of these 13 were given the breast only and 23 were obliged to give cows' milk in addition. "During the hot weather Nestle's condensed milk was largely given in preference to cows' milk owing to the lack of cool storage room in the poorer houses; in not more than one or two instances did we find a larder with outside ventilation. Several mothers gave cows' milk during the day and condensed milk at night. Nearly all those giving cows' milk were following the directions contained in the pamphlets distributed. "In Tooting, tube bottles were more largely used than the boat shaped, in Wandsworth the reverse was the case. "As in 1905, we found that the weaning of the infants, especially during the winter months, was mainly due to the malnutrition of the mothers or to the necessity of their having to go out to work owing to the lack of employment among the men. "With regard to the employment of midwives, out of the 1,626 mothers visited 692 availed themselves of their 96 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. services. Of these, in Balham 30 were registered and 26 non-registered; in Tooting 180 were registered and 77 nonregistered; in Wandsworth 261 were registered (Fairfield 77, Springfield 184) and 118 non-registered (Fairfield 43, Springfield 75). "Great difficulty was experienced in tracing the illegitimate births; wrong addresses were frequently given, or the mothers removed as soon after the birth as possible. In all, 55 illegitimate children were visited (Balham seven, Tooting 26, Fairfield Ward four, Springfield Ward 18). Of these, seven died in Tooting and two in the Fairfield Ward of Wandsworth; 12 had removed after our first visit and we were unable to trace them. One only of the illegitimate infants was premature and five were delicate from birth. It will be seen that the majority of these children were born strong and healthy, and yet the percentage of deaths is very high. One of the probable causes of this mortality is the putting of the children out to nurse; 11 of the babies were tended by strangers, and 10 were looked after by the grandmother, in each case necessitating the exposure of the infant to the early morning and night air. "The Diarrhoea epidemic in July, August and September was exceptionally severe, and we are glad to state that out of the 1,626 infants visited by us during the year only 29 succumbed to this disease. 34 died from other causes, chiefly respiratory complaints. A number of deaths were due to prematurity; many infants had died from this cause previous to our first call, and of those whom we did visit 37 were premature and 56 were delicate from birth. "Many sanitary defects were discovered in the course of our visits; these were remedied, or if involving structural defects, referred to you. In Balham 14 intimation notices were served, in Tooting 75, in Wandsworth 99 (Southfield one, Springfield 57, Fairfield 41). One statutory notice was required in Tooting and 12 in Wandsworth (Springfield five, Fairfield seven). "We realise that we have only been able to touch the fringe of the vast problem of how to help the mothers to a Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 97 better knowledge of the right care of their children. The improvement may be slow, almost imperceptible, but we feel that steady progress is being made, and we trust that next year better results will be seen." TABLE XLV. INFANTS VISITED. Balham. There is no doubt that the work has been most beneficial, but it is impossible, with the present staff, to extend it to the other Wards in the Borough. Some voluntary help was obtained towards the end of the year, and this partly accounts for the increase in the number of infants visited. This help was found to be most useful, and if possible, some additional help will be obtained in the current year. During the year 74 cases of this disease were notified by medical practitioners, four from Clapham, two from Putney, four from Streatham, one from Tooting, and 63 from Wandsworth. Two of these cases were under one year, two from one to five years, four from five to 15 years, seven from 15 to 25 years, 58 from 25 to 65 years, and one over 65 years. In 10 of the cases no enquiries were made, nine were notified from the Workhouse, while of the remaining 55 which were visited by the Inspectors, 30 died during the year, 13 removed to other addresses, and eight recovered, leaving only four still under observation at the end of the year. Of the 57 cases on the Register at the end of 1905, 13 died, 20 removed to other places, 12 recovered, and in seven no enquiries were made, leaving five still on the Register. The total number on the Register and under observation at the end of 1906 was nine. In 16 of the 55 cases visited for the first time during the year, a definite history of infection by a previous case was obtained. During the year disinfection was carried out at 134 premises, either after the death or removal of the patient. 28 of the premises disinfected were in Clapham, nine in Putney, 33 in Streatham, 14 in Tooting, and 50 in Wandsworth. 109 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. In the Public Institutions in the Borough 771 deaths occurred during the year, compared with 729 in 1905, and 669 in 1904. Of these 611 deaths were of persons not belonging to the Borough, compared with 61o in 1905, and 160 of persons belonging to the Borough, compared with 119 in 1905. The increase was due almost wholly to the increase in the number of deaths of persons belonging to the Borough. In Clapham 56 deaths occurred in the Hostel of God, 10 of these being Borough residents. Of these 34 were due to Cancer, 16 to Tubercular disease of the Lungs, three from other Tubercular diseases, and three from other causes. Five deaths occurred in the Priory, Roehampton, three from diseases of the Respiratory Organs, and two from other causes. In Streatham there were 10 deaths in St. Anne's Home and nine in the Middlesex County Asylum Annexe. Of the 10 in St. Anne's Home three were from diseases of the Respiratory Organs, one from Old Age, one from Obstruction of the Bowels, one from Accidental and four from other causes. Of the nine in the Middlesex County Asylum Annexe six were from Tubercular disease of the Lungs, one from Erysipelas, one from Acute Bronchitis, and one from other causes. In Tooting 493 deaths occurred in Public Institutions, 124 of which were of persons belonging to the Borough. Of the 229 deaths in Tooting Bee Asylum 119 were from Old Age, 17 from Tubercular disease of the Lungs, 17 from diseases of the Respiratory Organs, 13 from Inflammation and Softening of the Brain, eight from Cancer, seven from Bright's disease, six from Accident, and 42 from all other causes. In the Fever Hospitals 149 deaths occurred, 55 of which belonged to the Borough, compared with 50 in 1905, and 106 in 1904. 59 of these deaths were from Scarlet Fever, 61 from 116 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. theria, 13 from Enteric Fever, one from Measles, one from Whooping Cough, and 14 from all other causes. 115 deaths occurred in the Tooting Home, 60 of which were Borough residents. 49 of these were from Tubercular disease of the Lungs, 31 from Old Age, 11 from diseases of the Respiratory Organs, eight from Organic disease of the Heart, and 16 from all other causes. In Wandsworth 105 deaths occurred in the Middlesex County Asylum, 13 in the Royal Hospital for Incurables, eight in the Prison, and eight in the Workhouse. Of the 105 in the Middlesex County Asylum, 21 were from Tubercular disease of the Lungs, 18 from Old Age, 12 from diseases of the Respiratory Organs, four from Bright's disease, three from Accident, two from Enteritis, one from Erysipelas, two from other forms of Tubercular diseases, two from Softening of the Brain, and 40 from other causes, chiefly diseases of the Nervous System. Of the 13 deaths in the Royal Hospital for Incurables, three were from Old Age, one from Bright's disease, and eight from all other causes. Of the eight deaths in the Prison, two were from Bronchitis, and one each from Tubercular disease of the Lungs, Alcoholism, Pneumonia, Accident, Suicide, and Execution. Of the eight in the Workhouse, four were from Premature Birth and Developmental diseases, two from Old Age, and one each from disease of the Heart and Bright's disease. In other places not Institutions 64 deaths occurred, 16 of these being from Accidents, nine from Suicide, three from Tubercular disease of the Lungs, four from disease of the Heart, four from diseases of the Respiratory Organs, two each from Influenza, Cancer, Old Age, and Developmental diseases, and 20 from all other causes. 117 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Deaths of Illegitimate Children. 82 deaths of illegitimate children were registered during the year, 11 in Clapham, five in Putney, 22 in Streatham, 15 in Tooting, and 29 in Wandsworth. 23 of the deaths occurred from Premature Birth and Developmental diseases, 20 from Diarrhoea, 17 from Enteritis, four from Congenital Syphilis, two from Whooping Cough, two from Accident, and 24 from other causes. There has been a decided increase in the number of deaths of illegitimate children compared with 1905 when it was 69, but this is due wholly to the increase in the number of deaths from Diarrhoea and Enteritis compared with the previous year. The quality of the water was stated to be good in both the supplies in each month of the year, although in January, February, November and December the amount of Albuminoid Ammonia and the Oxygen required to oxidise were high. In the Lambeth District Supply a trace of free Ammonia was reported in January and December, and in the Southwark District Supply in December. Although the chemical results were on the whole satisfactory, yet the bacteriological results were not quite so satisfactory. In January the Director of Water Examinations reported that compared with December, 1905, the total number of the microbes was greater, and the Bacillus Coli results (both quantitative and qualitative) less satisfactory, the filtered waters containing about twice as many bacteria. In November he stated that chemically the Lambeth and Southwark filtered waters were open to objection as judged by the colour and the oxygen absorbed, and that bacteriologically the deterioration of the raw and filtered waters was still more marked. In the month of December he reported that the raw unfiltered water gave a satisfactory result from a bacteriological point of view, yet the filtered waters gave better results than in November. The Director reported that the Southwark and Vauxhall water yielded the worst results. He specially noted that the Chelsea water also taken from the Thames was, from a bacteriological point of view, satisfactory, but that the Southwark and Vauxhall was unsatisfactory. He explains this by the fact that at the Chelsea works the sand on the filter beds is much above the average in thickness. Owing to complaints received in the month of February the Public Health Committee gave instructions for samples of water to be analysed, but such analyses did not materially differ from those published in the Reports of the Metropolitan Water Board. In November analyses were made of two wells in the district. In one the water was badly polluted and quite unfit for use. The owner of the premises was warned and the Company's water was 121 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. laid on. In another case the water was fairly good, but a bacteriological examination was made which showed that although the Bacillus Coli was present, it was present in such small amount that I could not advise the Committee to order the well to be closed. Cutting-off Notices. During the year 138 notices were received from the Water Board stating that the water supply had been withdrawn. 104 of these notices referred to premises in the Lambeth District Supply, and 34 in the Southwark District Supply. Eight of these premises were in Clapham, nine in Putney, 74 in Streatham, 16 in Tooting, and 31 in Wandsworth. The supply was withdrawn for non-payment of rates from 37 premises, for defects from 39 premises, in 38 the premises were empty, in 22 the cause of the supply being withdrawn was not stated, and in two it was withdrawn by request. Mortuaries. No change has taken place in the four Mortuaries during the year. The Mortuary at Tooting has been thoroughly overhauled, a new concrete floor having been laid down, and a window and screen constructed so that bodies may be viewed without entering the Mortuary chamber. The plans for the new Mortuary at Wandsworth have been prepared, but the necessary land is not yet available. It is hoped that during the current year the erection of the Mortuary will be proceeded with. In Clapham 29 bodies were removed to the Mortuary, in Streatham 35, in Tooting 20, and in Wandsworth 128, of which 31 were from Putney and 11 from Tooting, a total of 212, compared with 188 in 1905. I have also to report that the Regulations and By-laws made by the Council in 1905 have worked very satisfactorily and that no complaints were received by me as to the staff employed there. 122 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. General Sanitary Work in 1906. The following list shows the special inspections made by me during the year :— (1.) Inspection of drains of non-provided schools for the certificate required by the London County Council. (a) Nightingale Square Roman Catholic School. Drains tested with water and certificate granted. (b) Macaulay Road School, Clapham. Drains and water closets tested with water and certificate granted. (c) Rectory Grove School, Clapham. Drains tested with water with satisfactory result. Water closets defective, but to be reconstructed. (d) St. Mary's Convent School, Clapham. Drains tested with water after reconstruction. (e) St. Leonard's Schools, Streatham. Drains tested with water and certificate granted. (f) Charlwood Road Schools, Putney. All drains water tested and certificate granted. (g) All Saints' School, Putney. Drains tested with water and certificate granted. (h) Holy Trinity Schools, Balham. Drains tested and sanitary arrangements reconstructed. (i) Holy Trinity School, Lutheran Place, Streatham. Drains partially reconstructed. (k) St. Joseph's Roman Catholic School, Wandsworth. Drains tested with water and found satisfactory. (2.) By request of the Licensing Justices the Council instructed me to inspect and report on the following Public Houses, and also to give evidence before the Compensation Committee at the Clerkenwell Sessions :— "The Antelope," High Street, Wandsworth. "The Velocipede," High Street, Wandsworth. "The Volunteer," The Plain, Wandsworth. 123 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. "The Prince of Wales," Lydden Road, Wandsworth. "The Old House at Home," Dunts Hill, Wandsworth. "The British Lion," Putney Bridge Road, Wandsworth. "The Coopers' Arms," High Street, Putney. "The Bricklayers' Arms," River Street, Putney. "The Railway Bell," Mitcham Road, Tooting. "The Fox," High Street, Tooting. (3.) Special inspections were made of the River Wandle in consequence of complaints of the presence of quantities of sewage fungus in the river and the smell arising therefrom. On inspecting in July I found that the river was in a foul condition owing to the presence of the fungus and that this fungus originated from the outfall of the Croydon Rural Sanitary Authority. That Authority have now reinstated the coke filters and cleansed that part of the river at the outfall, with the result that the nuisance has been to a considerable extent abated. (4.) Special inspections were made of Cubitt's Cottages, the houses in Point Pleasant, Wandsworth, and in Floss Street, Putney, with regard to their sanitary condition. The necessary notices were served and all have been or are now being complied with. (5.) With reference to the houses in The Platt, Putney, of which several inspections were made, instructions were given to the Solicitor that a closing order under the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, should be obtained. The magistrate on hearing the position of the defendant with regard to title, adjourned the summonses, and these have not yet been again heard. There is no doubt, however, but that the owner will either comply with the requirements of the Council by carrying out extensive repairs, or by rebuilding the houses. The premises were inspected by a Sub-Committee of the Public Health Committee before a closing order was applied for. 124 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. (6.) A Sub-Committee also inspected a number of houses in Garratt Lane known in the past as Jubilee Cottages, but now numbered in Garratt Lane. These properties have for many years been inspected by the Sanitary Inspector and myself yearly, but they have recently become so structurally defective that it was necessary to take steps to close some of them. Notices were served and as a result four of them were closed by the owners, and in the others the necessary repairs are being gradually done. (7.) Special inspections were made during the summer months of several localities, chiefly in Streatham, Balham, Southfields, Wandsworth, Totterdown, Tooting, and various parts of Putney, in consequence of complaints of residents in these localities as to smell from sewer ventilators. These complaints were more numerous in 1906, as a result of the continued dry weather, and a consequent absence of the natural flushing of the gullies, drains, and main sewers. In all 159 complaints were received, from Clapham North Ward four, Clapham South Ward 19, Putney 17, Balham 28, Streatham 29, Tooting 30, Southfield 29, Fairfield five, and Springfield eight. All these complaints were forwarded to the Surveyors who periodically reported that the necessary flushing and cleansing or other work had been done. (8.) With regard to the mews and passages mentioned in previous Reports I have made several inspections of these during the year. (a) The mews at rear of houses in York Road, worth, has been paved by the owners, and the place is now in a satisfactory condition. (b) The passage in the rear of Stanthorpe Road, ham, has been kept cleansed by the owners, but nothing structural has been carried out owing to 125 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. some legal difficulty as to the ownership and responsibility. (c) Simrose Court, Wandsworth, has been repaved by the owners and the houses in the Court rebuilt. Other passages and mews have been inspected by myself and by the Sanitary Inspectors and the necessary notices served, all of which were forthwith complied with. In addition to the above special inspections and the routine inspections of Bakehouses, Slaughterhouses, and Cowsheds, 205 inspections were made of other premises, chiefly previous to the service of statutory notices. My attendance was necessary at the Police Court on nine, at the High Court on two, at the County Court on two, and at the Licensing Sessions on two occasions during the year. Revenue Act. During the year 10 certificates were granted under the above Act and two refused. The certificates granted referred to 160 houses, or 246 separate tenements, and the two refused to nine houses, or 18 tenements. Sanitary Operations. The Table of Sanitary operations shows in detail the amount -and nature of the work carried out by the Sanftary Inspectors during the year. The number of complaints received and attended to was 1,595, compared with 1,357 in 1905, and 1,491 in 1904. There was an increase in both Wards in Clapham, a slight increase in Putney, a decrease in Streatham, an increase in Balham, an increase in Tooting, an increase in Southfield, and a decrease in Fairfield and Springfield Wards, compared with the previous year. The number of houses and premises inspected was 24,458, -compared with 22,383 in 1905, and 17,999 in 1904. 126 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The principal increases were in the Balham, Tooting, and Springfield Wards. The total number of house-to-house inspections was 5,686, compared with 6,290 in 1905, and 6,440 in 1904. There has been a slight increase in the number of house-tohouse inspections in Clapham North Ward, Putney and Tooting, but a decrease in the other Wards. This decrease is due to the pressure of other work, the increase in the number of complaints,, and the increase in the necessary clerical work. 127 Report of the Medical Officer of Health.
