The number of intimation notices served was 7,500, compared with 7,207 in 1905, and of statutory notices 1,044, compared with 1,099 in 1905. The greatest number of preliminary notices was served in the Balham and Fairfield Wards, and the smallest in Southfield and Streatham Wards. The greatest number of statutory notices was served in Balham and Springfield Wards, and the smallest number in Putney and Streatham Wards. With regard to Putney, although 31 statutory notices were served only five were complied with, and this is due to the statutory notices which were served on the owners of The Piatt, which are at present in the Solicitor's hands. With regard to the number in Southfield Ward not complied with, 20 of these were with respect to property in Point Pleasant, the work at which is now in hand. Of the 117 statutory notices not complied with, the majority are in hand and have been complied with in the early months of the current year. The number of statutory notices served include by-law notices, 296 of which were served in respect of the following contraventions of the by-laws : yard paving 88, cleansing house and other contraventions of by-laws as to houses let in lodgings 70, repairs to water closets 59, repairs to flushing apparatus of water closets 32, covers to cisterns 29, cleansing cisterns 16, paving and ing stables one, and repairs to manure receptacle one. The number of cases of overcrowding dealt with during the year was 100, compared with 92 in 1905. Seven of these were in Clapham, five in Putney, 11 in Streatham, 25 in Tooting, and 52 in Wandsworth. There has been a decided increase in the number of cases dealt with in Tooting compared with previous years, due to the poverty of the people residing in one or two streets in that sub-district. In Wandsworth the overcrowding was chiefly confined to the Fairfield and Springfield Wards. All these cases were remedied after service of preliminary notice with the exception of one in Clapham, where it was necessary to issue a summons. 1,219 ashpits were provided or repaired, compared with 1,128 in 1905 and 1,166 in 1904; and 574 complaints with reference to the non-removal of house refuse (62 from Clapham, 92 from Putney, 156 from Streatham, 36 from Tooting, and 219 from Wandsworth), were received during the year. These complaints were, as in previous years, at once forwarded to the Surveyors and attended to by the Dusting Inspectors. Removal of House Refuse. No change has during the year taken place in the methods adopted for the removal of house refuse. The refuse is removed weekly, but in some instances, as in flats, the refuse is removed twice in each week. As regards disposal the methods are the same as in previous years, viz., in the North Ward of Clapham and part of the South Ward, as also in a small portion of lower Streatham, the refuse is sent by rail to shoots in the country. From a portion of Clapham South, Streatham, Balham and Tooting, it is disposed of at the Destructor at Tooting. In Wandsworth and Putney the dust is discharged into barges at Putney and Bell Lane, Wandsworth, and deposited on the low-lying banks of the lower reaches of the Thames. During the 12 months ending March, 1906, 11,789 tons were sent by rail, 19,595 tons were disposed of in the Destructor, and 22,162 tons were barged, a total of 53,546 tons. The total cost of collection and disposal for the same period was £16,923. 183 statutory notices were served under Section 37 of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, to provide ashpits in accordance with the by-laws and these notices were complied with. 1,094 yards in connection with dwelling-houses and 68 in connection with stables were paved, drained or cleansed, compared with 1,023 and 62 in 1905. 4,954 rooms and 1,038 staircases and passages were cleansed or repaired, compared with 5,565 and 923 in 1905. 141 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 142 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The decrease in the number of rooms cleansed is due to the decrease in the number of house-to-house inspections. The greatest number of rooms cleansed was in Balham Ward, where 1,138 were done, and the smallest number in Southfield and Putney. 1,107 drains were cleansed or repaired and 109 rain-water pipes disconnected from drains. 185 notices of reconstruction of drains were sent on to the Surveyors from this department:—33 in Clapham, 22 in Putney, 77 in Streatham, eight in Tooting, and 45 in Wandsworth. The number of water closets cleansed and repaired was 1,936, compared with 2,141 in 1905, 1,979 in 1904 and 1,783 in 1903. 253 accumulations of manure were removed after service of notice:—25 in Clapham, 44 in Putney, 86 in Streatham, 46 in Tooting, and 52 in Wandsworth. Compared with 1905 there has been a considerable reduction in the number of notices served for abatement of this nuisance. The greatest reduction occurred in Putney, while in Tooting there was a slight increase. 16 notices were served for the removal of manure every 48 hours, compared with 20 in 1905, and 15 in 1904. 842 water cisterns were cleansed, covered or repaired, 288 in Clapham, 68 in Putney, 228 in Streatham, 50 in Tooting, and 208 in Wandsworth, compared with 823 in 1905. 178 premises were inspected and notices served where animals were kept in such a condition or in such a manner as to be a nuisance. 16 of these were in Clapham, seven in Putney, 24 in Streatham, 12 in Tooting, and 119 in Wandsworth. As in former years this nuisance was most common in the sub-district of Wandsworth, and particularly in the Fairfield and Springfield Wards, and the animals kept were in nearly all the cases ducks or hens. 143 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 128 smoke nuisances were reported during the year, five in Clapham, seven in Putney, 10 in Streatham, two in Tooting, and 104 in Wandsworth, 95 of which were in the Fairfield Ward. Proceedings were taken in eight cases and in five fines amounting to £16 10s. were inflicted with 17s. costs, while in two cases orders were made and served with 6s. costs in each case. 11 complaints of black smoke were received from the Public Control Department of the London County Council with regard to 13 premises, and 13 complaints from the Coal Smoke Abatement Society with regard to 22 premises. These were all at once attended to by the Inspectors. 184 soil pipes were repaired, 17 in Clapham, 17 in Putney, 94 in Streatham, three in Tooting, and 53 in Wandsworth, compared ■with 175 in 1905, and 269 in 1904. The number of unclassified nuisances was 893, 278 in Clapham, 136 in Putney, 134 in Streatham, 132 in Tooting, and 213 in Wandsworth, compared with 772 in 1905. Disinfection. The total number of rooms disinfected after the occurrence of cases of infectious diseases was 2,794 (4/6 in Clapham, 143 in Putney, 735 in Streatham, 498 in Tooting, and 942 in Wandsworth), compared with 2,947 in 1905 and 2,527 in 1904. The decrease was due to the decrease in the number of cases •of Scarlet Fever. 1,669 lotes of bedding were stoved at the Disinfecting Station, compared with 1,778 in 1905, and 1,129 in 1904 The 1,669 lots of bedding included the following:—1,165 beds, 1,200 bolsters, 3,043 pillows, 1,285 mattresses, 752 palliasses, 2,383 sheets, 1,790 quilts, 929 cushions, 7,429 articles of clothing, 3,971 blankets, 670 carpets, and 3,269 extra articles, a total of 28,885 articles, compared with 30,477 in 1905, and 20,295 in 1904. 3 144 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 902 premises were disinfected after Measles, and 134 after Tubercular disease of the Lungs, compared with 798 and 161 in 1905. 33 beds, five bolsters, 19 pillows, 34 mattresses, 50 palliasses, nine sheets, four quilts, 13 cushions, 103 articles of clothing, seven blankets, 43 carpets, and 142 extra articles, a total of 465 articles, were removed and destroyed by request of the owners during the year. Disinfection was also carried out by the Sanitary Staff at 15 premises after non-notifiable diseases, chiefly Cancer, and the sum of £7 iis. 4d. was received for such disinfection. The disinfection by steam was carried out at the Station at Tooting, and I am pleased to report that this Station has proved most satisfactory in every way. I have made a considerable number of visits to the Station during the year, and am able to report that the work has been carried out by the staff to my entire satisfaction. At the close of 1905 there were 302 milkshops on the Register, 73 have been added in 1906 and 23 removed, leaving 352 on the Register at the end of 1906. During the year 805 inspections were made of these premises and 35 notices, chiefly for cleansing, were served. Two prosecutions were taken for occupying premises without being registered and in all the cases fines were inflicted. Special attention has again been given this year to the enforcing of the Milkshop Regulations, and the notice of all registered purveyors of milk was called to the regulations which deal with the protection of milk against infection or contamination. Two milk vendors were prosecuted for failing to comply with the regulations in that the vessel used for storage on the counter was not covered so as to preserve the purity of the milk against infection or contamination. These milk vendors were convicted and fined in one case 10s. and 2s. costs, and in the other £1 and 2s. costs. Cowsheds. The above premises have been entirely reconstructed and new appliances installed. The system of ventilation, however, has not worked successfully, but alterations are being made which will eventually improve matters, and prevent the occurrence of any nuisance from the escape of offensive effluvia. Offensive Trades. The following is a list of offensive trades carried on in the Borough :—• (1.) Harrison, Barber & Company, Garratt Lane, Wansworth, Knacker's Yard Do. do. Bone Boilers. Do. do. Manure Manufacturers. (2.) W. C. Milestone, Bendon Valley, Wandsworth, Fat Melter. 154 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. (3.) Wickens, Pease & Co., Garratt Lane, Wandsworth, Bone Boiler. Do. do. Tallow Melters. (4.) James Ford, Bendon Valley, Wandsworth Fat Melter. (5.) Dallett & Company, High Street, Putney Soap Boilers. Do. do. Tallow Melters. With regard to No. 1, complaint was made by the Sanitary Inspector that the effluvia from the works were smelt in Garratt Lane, and that on inspecting he found that the furnace used for destroying these effluvia was not burning. Notice was at once served. On the 21 st June the Inspector again reported that there was an offensive smell from these works in Garratt Lane, and on inspecting he found that the doors of the slaughter-house had been left open and the offensive effluvia were escaping into the open air. On the 3rd July the Inspector reported that the furnace used to destroy the offensive effluvia was out, and the offensive effluvia were escaping without being destroyed. On the 23rd July the Inspector again reported a contravention of the By-laws in that the effluvia from the mixing tank chamber, which receives the residues from the boiling room were escaping into the open air causing offensive smell in the immediate neighbourhood. On the attention of the firm being called to this, structural works were carried out and the offensive vapours from the mixing tank chamber were carried by means of a ventilating tube to the furnace and there destroyed. In October the Inspector reported another contravention of the By-laws in regard to the storage of offensive matter in an open shed, and proceedings were taken against the firm, but the case was adjourned sine die, a written undertaking having been given to the effect that the shed would not be used for storage of offensive matter, and the firm agreed to pay £1 is. costs. With regard to No. 3, no complaint was received by me till the month of July, when on the 13th and 18th of that month the 155 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Inspector reported that the door of the boiling room was open and the offensive effluvia escaping, causing a nuisance in the neighbouring streets. No other complaint was received in regard to these works during the rest of the summer. In all 177 inspections were made during the year of the premises where these offensive trades were carried on, five notices were served, and in one proceedings were taken. Ice Cream Premises. At the end of 1905 there were 223 of these on the Register. During 1906, 32 were added and 26 removed, leaving a total of 229 on the Register. 368 inspections were made during the year and 33 notices were served, all of which were for cleansing. Houses let in Lodgings. At the end of 1905 there were 218 of these on the Register. None were added during the year. 438 inspections were made of these premises. 10 notices were served for overcrowding and 406 for other conditions, chiefly cleansing. With regard to 14 premises summonses were issued. In four of the cases a fine of £3 with 6s. costs was inflicted, in seven a fine of £1 15s. and 14s. costs, and in three the costs (6s.) were paid, but no penalty was inflicted. On the whole the new By-laws have proved satisfactory, although some owners still fail to realise that the necessary cleansing must be carried out at the specified time. Offensive Trade Refuse. The arrangement entered into by the Council for the removal of the above by a contractor still continues to work satisfactorily. The only alteration made in the contract this year was that the minimum charge should be 2s. instead of is. as at present. 156 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Unsound Food. During the year three boxes and 10 skate, three boxes of haddocks, three cases and six codfish, one box monkfish, one box roes, one trunk of plaice, one trunk of mackerel, nine boxes of kippers, one kit of coal fish, two barrels of sprats, 20 crabs, 12 rabbits, six score of lettuce, nine crates of bananas and one basket of plums, were destroyed at the request of the owners and the usual certificates granted. In one case proceedings were taken for exposing for sale and selling sausage rolls unfit for food, and a fine of £10 was inflicted with 2s. costs. Canned Foods. In accordance with the instruction of the Council the following report was made:— "14 samples of canned goods have been submitted for analysis, chiefly to ascertain if any preservative was used in the manufacture. Five of the cans contained corned beef, and the nine others—galantine of chicken and tongue, potted tongue, potted beef, potted ham, minced steak, ham loaf, roast beef, beefsteak and onions, and jellied brawn respectively. The samples were purchased in various parts of the Borough, and chiefly in the poorer localities. The Inspector who purchased the samples procured the oldest specimens possible, and that may account for the presence of tin in 10 out of the 14 samples. "All the samples with the exception of one were derived from the United States. The maximum amount of tin found in the samples only amounted to about 0.14 of a grain per 1 lb., not sufficient to be injurious to health. 157 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 'The analyst reports on his examination as follows:— 'The method of procedure with each can consisted in opening and completely removing the contents, which, after being pulped and thoroughly mixed were examined for preservatives and metallic contamination, and in all likely cases aniline dye or other added colouring matter. The preservatives searched for were (1) Boric acid and borax, (2) Sulphites, (3) Formaldehyde, (4) Salicylic and (5) Benzoic Acids. The condition of the interior of the can was also noted together with the manner in which the soldering had been effected, i.e., whether soldered internally or externally. 'With regard to the results obtained, it will be seen that in every sample the contents of the can were sound and in no case were preservatives or added colouring matter detected. 'Evidence of dirt or other extraneous matter was carefully looked for, but in every instance the samples were in this respect satisfactory. 'In only three cases, namely, A, G and N, did the amount of metallic contamination by tin reach "heavy traces." In the remainder it was entirely absent or present only "in traces," and as such could scarcely be regarded as dangerous to health.' " 158 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The following resolutions passed by the Incorporated Society of Medical Officers of Health on the subject of tinned and preserved foods were laid before the Committee and considered:— "That in the opinion of the Metropolitan Branch of the Incorporated Society of Medical Officers of Health, it is necessary in order to secure better protection for the public in regard to tinned and preserved foods, that legislation should be passed embodying the following important reforms:— "(1.) That the name and address of the manufacturer and the date of canning should be impressed on the cans. "(2.) It shall be unlawful to sell for any purpose canned goods that are unfit for human consumption, and it should be the duty of any person in possession of unsound canned foods to notify and surrender same to the local authority, who should destroy such unsound food free of charge. "(3.) 'A certain number of tins (say one per cent.) from each consignment should be opened on importation and the contents examined under the supervision or instructions of the sanitary authority concerned prior to such canned foods being placed on the market. "(4.) Tins which are known in the trade as 'doubtfuls' should be deemed to be unfit for human consumption. "(5.) Food intended for canning should be inspected by independent officials prior to being canned, and all foods should be canned under strictly hygienic conditions. "(6.) In the opinion of the Branch the use of preservatives in canned food is unnecessary and should under no circumstances be permitted," stating that such resolutions have been forwarded to the Local Government Board, the Board of Agriculture, the London County Council, and the City and Borough Councils, and expressing the hope that the Council will support the Branch by making representations to those bodies, with the view of obtaining the desired reform. 160 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The Committee recommended that the Council agree with the foregoing resolutions except that the number of tins opened should not be so great as one per cent., and that communications be addressed to the Local Government Board, the Board of Agriculture, and the London County Council in support thereof. Factories and Workshops. I am able to report that there has been a considerable increase in the amount of work done by the Inspectors during the year, and that, on the whole, the condition of all the workshops in the Borough is most satisfactory. With the exception of the work in regard to outworkers no difficulties have been noted, and the difficulty with regard to outworkers has been largely due to the great lessening of the amount of work done by these outworkers, owing either to a decline in trade, or that work is more generally being carried out in large factories or workshops, or to both these factors combined. The Tables which follow are similar to those used in previous Reports, and the whole of the work done by both the male and female Inspectors is there summarised in a concise form. The total number of workshops on the Register was 1,719, including 161 retail bakehouses, 119 workshop laundries, 293 domestic workshops and 1,146 other workshops, but not including 253 factories, 891 workplaces, and 233 outworkers' premises. Including all factories, workshops, workplaces, and outworkers' premises, there were on the Register at the end of 1906, 3,096 premises, compared with 3,045 in 1905, 2,987 in 1904, and 2,676 in 1903. The total number of inspections made during the year was 5,997, compared with 4,933 in 1905, and 4,946 in 1904. The total number of notices served was 427, 399 intimation and 28 statutory, compared with 462 in 1905, and 465 in 1904. There were two prosecutions, one for failing to cleanse a workshop and the other for sanitary defects, and in both fines -were inflicted. i6i Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Under the head of other workshops are included domestic workshops, and under the head of places where food is prepared for sale are included the kitchens of hotels, restaurants, tripe, fried fish and eel shops, and other places where food is prepared for sale. There was a marked increase in the number of inspections of factories during the year, especially of factory laundries and other factories. In all 658 inspections were made of 253 factories and 69 notices, all of these being intimation notices, were served. It is satisfactory to note that no statutory notices were necessary during the year. The total number of workshops on the Register was 1,719, 119 of which were workshop laundries, 161 workshop bakehouses, and 1,439 other workshops. In all 3,233 inspections of these were made, compared with 2,628 in 1905, and 238 intimation and 19 statutory notices were served. The total number of workplaces on the Register was 891, and 1,863 inspections were made, while 119 intimation and seven statutory notices were served. The total number of defects found during the year was 558, including 19 cases in which there was a breach of special sanitary requirements for bakehouses. To abate these nuisances 399 intimation and 28 statutory notices were served, all of which, with the exception of 12, were complied with. These 12 notices were served in the latter part of December and were complied with in the early part of the current year. In all 558 defects were found and 537 remedied, but it was only necessary to serve 399 intimation notices, as one notice frequently called atention to several defects on the same premises. In the case of the statutory notices served intimation notices had previously been served. 162 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. INSPECTION OF FACTORIES, WORKSHOPS AND WORKPLACES. table lxxii. With regard to the foregoing Table which differs somewhat from that which appeared in previous Reports, it will be seen by comparison that the number of outworkers shows a large reduction. There was, however, a slight increase in the addresses of outworkers received from other Boroughs, the numbers being in 1906, 173; and in 1905, 146. The total number of lists received twice a year was 12, containing the names of 113 outworkers, while 21 lists were received once in the year containing the names of 89 outworkers. 243 inspections were made of these premises and 22 defects were found necessitating the service of 14 notices, all of which were complied with. These notices were all for cleansing. The works carried on by these home workers are in this Borough almost wholly tailoring, dressmaking, hosiery making, and bootmaking and repairing. During the year six cases of infectious disease were notified' from the homes of outworkers, five of Scarlet Fever and one of Diphtheria, compared with five in 1905, and 12 in 1904. The following are the districts to which lists of outworkers were forwarded, and also the number of outworkers:—Battersea 27, Lambeth 19, Croydon Rural 16, Camberwell 14, Stepney 13, Fulham seven, East Ham five, Southwark three, Bermondsey, Ilford, Stoke Newington, Wimbledon, St. Pancras, and Chelsea two each, Islington, Poplar, Wetminster, Bethnal Green, Hampstead, and Ealing one each, a total of 122, compared with 231 in 1905, and 246 in 1904. 31 of the outworkers are on the workshop register and the premises were inspected as workshops. 100 of the outworkers were either unknown, removed, had. discontinued work, or had had none for many months. 167 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The Tables which follow summarise the work done during the year by the female Sanitary Inspectors. The total number of premises inspected was 1,971 (398 in Clapham, 235 in Putney, 663 in Streatham, 149 in Tooting, and 528 in Wandsworth). 151 inspections were made of factories, 1,125 workshops, 78 of domestic workshops, 472 of workplaces, and 145 of outworkers' premises, while 378 premises were visited for enquiry and no workroom found. There were only five cases of overcrowding discovered, compared with 12 in 1905, and 17 in 1904, and these were at once abated. Six were insufficiently ventilated, and 63 were found to be in a dirty condition. In one workshop there was no separate water closets for the sexes, while in nine factories and 11 workshops the water closets were defective. Other nuisances were discovered in two factories, 33 workshops, two domestic workshops, 20 workplaces, and nine outworkers' premises, and these have all been abated. It was only necessary to serve 121 intimation and nine statutory notices, while for the first time since the female Sanitary inspectors were appointed it was necessary to take proceedings for sanitary defects against the owner of premises occupied as a laundry. 55 workshops and 10 domestic workshops where females were employed were reported to H.M. Canal Boats Act. During the year 31 inspections of Canal Boats were made, but no notices were served. The condition of all the boats inspected was reported to be satisfactory. Statement of Work under the Sale of Food and Drugs Act. Before dealing with the routine work of the Public Health Department with regard to the Food and Drugs Act, some notice must be taken of an important Conference which was held at the Council House during the year. On the 20th June the Chairman of the Public Health Committee, Dr. Gay, brought before the Council the following motion which was adopted :— "That the Public Health Committee invite the registered milk vendors in the Borough to a Conference with the Committee to be held at the Council House or the Town Hall at a convenient date to consider :— "(1.) What methods can be adopted to provide that the milk supply to London might be properly protected from contamination at the time of milking and during transit. "(2.) Any representations that might be made to the proper authorities, if necessary, with the assistance of other Local Authorities in London to ensure the adoption of such methods. "(3.) Any other matters or suggestions that may contribute to the cleanly distribution of milk in London." The Conference was held at the Council House on the 17th July, and was largely attended by the vendors of milk in the Borough. The following resolutions were passed at the Conference :— "(1.) That the Board of Agriculture be urged to bring into force and carry out the Dairies, Cowsheds and Milkshops 177 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Order in the country, with such variations as are applicable in the various districts. (2.) That the Board of Agriculture be requested to take steps to ensure that the udders of the cows, and the hands and arms of the person milking, shall be clean at the time of milking, and that the vessel used shall be clean and be kept solely for the purpose of milking. (3.) That in the opinion of the Conference all railway companies carrying milk should provide proper insulated trucks, as now used by the London and North-Western Railway Company for the purpose. {4.) That the Board of Agriculture be urged to take steps to ensure the carriage of milk in sealed churns. (5.) That in the opinion of this Conference all dairies should be licensed annually, and only licensed dairies should sell milk for consumption on or off the premises, and that all milk on the counter for sale in open vessels should be covered with muslin or other suitable cover. (6.) It was generally admitted that preservatives are not necessary. We recommend, with a view to the delivery of milk in London free from dirt, that a copy of such resolutions be sent to the Board of Agriculture and the other Metropolitan Borough and City Councils and that they be asked, if they approve, to support such resolutions." The Metropolitan Borough Councils of Deptford and Battersea in reply stated that they had written to the Board of Agriculture in support of such resolutions; the Westminster City Council that it concurred in the suggestion, and that it was of opinion that it is desirable that sealed bottles should be substituted for the metal cans now in use for the retail delivery of milk; the Corporation of London and the Kensington Borough Council that they approved of the several resolutions with the exception of so much of resolution No. 5 as relates to licensing. 178 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. During the past year 905 samples of foods and drugs have been submitted to the Public Analyst compared with 853 in 1905. Of these, 84 or 9.2 per cent., were reported to be adulterated, compared with 7.6 in 1905, and 11.33 in 1904. The percentage of milk samples found to be adulterated was io'26, as compared with 7.2 in 1905, and 15.3 in 1903. It would, therefore, appear, judging by the results obtained, that the milk supply of the Borough has somewhat deteriorated; if, however, the tabulated results arrived at by the Public Analyst be carefully examined it will be noticed that in many cases the adulteration is extremely small, so much so indeed that in 20 cases of adulterated milk samples which were reported to the Public Health Committee on account of this it was decided that no action should be taken against the vendor. In 59 cases out of the 84 adulterated samples detected, proceedings were instituted against the vendors. The total amount received in penalties under the above Acts during the year amounted to £100 18s. 6d., and the costs recovered £39 13s. 6d., making a grand total of £140 12s., compared with £123 16s. 6d. in 1905. 179 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. TABLE LXXXV. Year. No. of Samples lake 11. No. Adulterated. Percentage Adulterated. 1897 597 25 4.1 1898 606 26 4.2 1899 602 50 8.3 1900 587 43 7.1 1901 600 69 11.5 1902 584 51 8.7 1903 729 90 12.3 1904 750 85 11.3 1905 853 65 7.6 1906 905 84 9.2 As has been pointed out in previous Reports, the percentage of adulterations detected can never be relied upon as an absolute proof of the purity or otherwise of the food supply of a district, but can, at best, be used only as a rough indication. Although, therefore, the percentage of adulterated samples shows an increase over that of last year, it must not be concluded that the purity of the food supply has deteriorated to that extent. Such a result may be arrived at in many ways; one of which and indeed the most important being the methods adopted in procuring the samples. During the whole of the past year the Inspector has had an allowance in order that he might engage his own assistants to procure samples. This arrangement has enabled him to change them frequently, and works admirably, and probably the result shown in the above Table is in great measure due to this cause. 183 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The Inspector nas had during the year five females and three male assistants at his disposal, each of whom appears to be a suitable type of person to be employed in such work. In this respect the Inspector has been fortunate, for it is a matter of experience that it is very difficult to find suitable persons desirous of doing the work, and occasionally, although 185 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. willing, they are poor witnesses at subsequent Police Court proceedings and break down under cross-examination. Much the same methods have been adopted in procuring samples as in previous years, and in many cases where it has been difficult and impossible to detect fraud in any other way a female assistant has procured various articles of grocery, etc., at the shops in question, and, having made herself known to the tradesmen as a regular customer can then procure samples of butter, coffee, etc., for analysis, which will be the same article as "ordinarily" supplied, and this frequently proved to be adullerated. The art of substituting adulterated for the genuine articles has reached a very high pitch of efficiency, and the Inspector reports to me that while he is endeavouring to do his utmost to secure the conviction of unscrupulous traders, there are many "suspects" in the Borough, shops where he is morally certain fraudulent methods are practised. In such cases It becomes necessary to spend a considerable amount of time and attention, frequently having to keep such premises under observation for months. As an example of this I instance the following case which occurred during the year: "A" is a trader in a good class neighbourhood of the Borough, and during the last four years the Inspector has purchased many samples of butter from his shop, employing a female assistant. On each occasion the sample has been returned by the Analyst as genuine. During the year, however, information reached the Inspector from a very reliable source that a considerable amount of margarine was being substituted for butter to regular customers at this particular shop. It was necessary to find a suitable woman living in the neighbourhood who was able and willing to assist. This, a matter of no inconsiderable difficulty, accomplished, she was instructed to call three or four times weekly for various articles, butter included, and she continued so doing for over two months. The Inspector on the last occasion accompanied her and took an official sample, with the result it was found to contain 98 per cent, of margarine. Another instance of fraud which the Inspector was able to detect during the year, which also illustrates the "smartness" of these fraudulent traders, is as follows: "B," a resident at a 186 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. private house in the Borough, employs three or four men for a fortnight to make a house-to-house canvass in certain districts, purporting to sell pure Dorset butter at one shilling per pound, stating that their master has it made on his own farm and sends it up to London twice weekly. It seems strange that purchasers should believe that pure Dorset butter can be sold at such a price, but it is certain that in this particular way this man obtained a considerable number of customers. For some time the Inspector kept him under observation, following him day after day and watching and making a note of the addresses at which he called. The Inspector was surprised to find that the majority of this man's customers were of the upper middle class, and were not as he expected to find, confined to the poorer localities. Eventually the Inspector persuaded a man living in an adjoining house to one of those at which this trader now called, to purchase a sample for analysis, with the result that it was found to contain 96 per cent, of margarine. The work done by the Inspector cannot be properly judged, either by the number of samples taken or by the percentage of adulteration detected, but only by the method employed and the interest shown by the responsible official entrusted with such work. It has been said that 25 per cent, of all butter sold is adulterated, and that only 10 per cent, of the whole, or 2.5 per cent, of the adulteration is detected, but I think that the Borough can lay claim to a somewhat improved percentage of pure butter supplied to its inhabitants. Much difficulty has also been experienced during the year in dealing with certain milk sellers in the Borough, principally small traders who sell two classes of milk, one Separated Milk and the other New Milk, both from the same barrow. The difficulties in this case are obvious, as one can be easily substituted for the other, and, indeed, at private houses where the milk is left on the doorsteps in cans this is done to a very large extent. In these 187 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. cases it is only possible to secure the conviction of an offender by the use of somewhat similar methods as those already described. On several occasions during the year the Inspector on searching barrows has discovered small hand cans containing water. When asked to account for this the invariable reply is that the water in question is used for washing and cleansing cans. Instances such as the above point to the fact that considerable amendment of the existing legislation is needed, giving further powers to executive officials. As has been pointed out in a previous Report, it should be made an offence to carry water on a barrow from which milk is sold, and again if Separated or Skimmed Milk is sold, then the receptacle from which it is sold should be painted a distinctive colour in order that a purchaser may be aware of the nature of the article. In one case only during the year was the "warranty" defence relied upon, with the result that the summons was dismissed. Although the Borough has been fortunate of late years in this respect, it cannot be pointed out too frequently that this method of defence has always to be anticipated, and proved a very serious obstruction in the effective working of the Acts. The case in which this "warranty" defence was pleaded was against a vendor in whose name two previous convictions had already been registered during the preceding 18 months. With regard to the giving of a notice in cases of admixture, it has been observed during the year that such notices, more particularly in cases of coffee and chicory, and chocolate powder, are drawn up and printed with a view to protecting the vendor against a conviction of fraud rather than with the intention of clearly setting forth to the purchaser the nature of such admixture. 188 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. In my opinion it should be made compulsory for the exact nature and composition (including the proportion of ingredients) to be clearly printed on the containing wrapper. Only in one instance has it been necessary to issue a summons against a milk vendor for refusing to sell a sample to the Inspector on demand. In conclusion, I am pleased to be able to report that the whole of the work of the department was carried out by the Inspectors and the Clerical Staff to my entire satisfaction. I have the honour to be, Mr. Mayor and Gentlemen, Your obedient Servant, P. CALDWELL SMITH, Medical Officer of Health. APPENDIX. All other.causes 65 35 30 9 1 7 5 30 13 42 All causes 417 216 201 141 41 18 11 137 69 126 ANNUAL STATEMENT Of the work done in the Borough of Wandsworth under the Sale of Food and Drugs Acts, summarised from his statutory quarterly reports by Dr. John Muter, Public Analyst. During the year 1906, the Inspector brought to the South London Central Public Laboratory 905 samples of Food and Drugs, classifiable as follows:— Dairy Produce 821 samples. Groceries 50 ” Drugs 17 ” Bread and Cereals 11 ” Alcoholic Beverages 3 ” Confectionery 2 ” Meats 1 ” 905 samples. I.—Dairy Produce. The following Table shows the nature and number of the different samples submitted, and the results of their analysis :— Milk 526 samples. Butter 287 ” Margarine 5 ” Lard 2 ” Cream 1” 821 samples. Of these, 748 were found genuine, while the following 73 were reported against:— N 198 Report—Public Analyst. Name of Article. Nature and extent of Impurity. Milk Case No. 65 8 per cent, extraneous water Milk ” 171 13 ” Milk ” 195 7 ” Milk (separated) ” 196 ” 19 Milk ” 206 3 ” Milk ” 237 3 ” Milk ” 286 4 ” Milk ” 365 11 ” Milk ” 385 10 ” Milk ” 395 9 ” Milk ” 488 4 ” Milk (separated) ” 600 10 ” Milk ” 604 2 ” Milk ” 605 4 ” Milk ” 620 6 ” Milk ” 621 13 ” Milk ” 696 5 ” Milk ” 781 5 ” Milk ” 809 2 ” Milk ” 837 6 ” Milk ” 50 30 per cent. milk fat deficient Milk ” 166 30 ” Milk ” 226 21 ” Milk ” 256 8 ” Milk ” 258 8 ” Milk ” 261 15 ” Milk ” 276 6 ” Milk ” 284 10 ” Milk ” 322 5 ” Milk ” 326 5 ” Milk ” 338 13 ” Milk ” 349 21 ” Milk ” 356 10 ” Milk ” 381 9 ” Milk ” 409 16 ” Milk ” 414 7 ” Milk ” 416 7 ” Milk ” 417 22 ” Milk ” 431 26 ” Milk ” 432 27 ” Milk ” 433 17 ” 199 Report—Public Analyst. Milk Case No. 444 6 per cent, milk fat deficient Milk ” 449 5 ” Milk ” 458 22 ” Milk ” 496 10 ” Milk ” 538 9 ” Milk ” 544 22 ” Milk ” 610 8 ” Milk ” 674 5 ” Milk ” 269 20 per cent, milk fat deficient and 5 per cent, extraneous water Milk ” 437 17 per cent, milk fat deficient and 5 per cent, extraneous water Milk ” 622 4.5 grains Boric Acid per pint Milk ” 722 2.8 ” Milk 726 15 ” Butter ” 53 96 per cent. Margarine Butter ” 68 37 ” Butter ” 88 97 ” Butter (on bread) ” 117 98 ” Butter (on bread) ” 126 98 ” Butter ” 159 95 ” Butter ” 249 91 ” Butter ” 268 90 ” Butter ” 541 94 ” Butter ” 545 93 ” Butter ” 579 94 ” Butter ” 737 56 ” Butter 751 96 ” Butter ” 771 90 ” Butter ” 785 90 ” Butter ” 786 96 ” Butter ” 853 90 ” Butter ” 898 98 ” Butter ” 758 4.3 per cent. excess of moisture These figures show that io'26 per cent, of the milk samples, and 6'62 per cent, of the butter samples, were adulterated. This indicates in the case of the milk samples a falling off from 7.25 per cent, adulteration in 1905; but in the case of the butter, an improvement, the percentage of impurity in 1905 being 8.96. 200 Report—Public Analyst. II.—Groceries. These included :— Coffee 33 samples. Mustard 7 ” Pepper 5 ” Cocoa 4 ” Vinegar 1 ” 50 samples. All the above were found genuine, with the exception of the following eight:— Coffee, Case No. 198 62 per cent. Chicory Coffee ” 303 45 ” Coffee ” 418 35 ” Coffee ” 428 80 ” Coffee ” 792 22 ” Coffee ” 826 40 ” Cocoa ” 477 37 per cent. sugar and 33 per cent. arrowroot Cocoa ” 479 35 per cent. sugar and 32 per cent. arrowroot This shows an increased percentage of adulteration from 8 69 in 1905 to 16 in 1906. III.—Drugs. Under this head were included:— Lime Water 7 samples. Iron Pills 3 ” Tincture of Iodine 2 ” Mercurial Ointment 2 ” Camphorated Oil 1 ” Licorice Powder 1 ” Linseed Oil 1 ” 17 samples. 201 Report—Public Analyst. All these were found genuine, with the following two exceptions:— Tincture of Iodine, Case No. 49 25 per cent, deficient in Iodine and Potassium Iodide Mercurial Ointment ” 850 64 per cent, deficient in Mercury These results indicate that 11.76 per cent, of the articles submitted in this division were adulterated. In 1905 all were genuine. IV.—Bread and Cereals. These included :— Bread 3 samples. Sago 3 ,, Arrowroot 2 ,, Flour 2 ,, Ground Rice 1 ,, 11 samples. All these were found genuine. V.—Alcoholic Beverages. In this division the following were examined :— Rum 2 samples. Whisky 1 ,, 3 samples. Of these, one sample of Rum and the one of Whisky were found genuine, while one sample of Rum was reported against, asunder :— Rum, Case No. 843 12 deg. below legal limit. This indicates a percentage of impurity amounting to 33.3. 202 Report—Public Analyst. VI.—Confectionery. One sample of Raspberry Jam and one of Ice Cream were analysed, and found genuine. In 1905, a similar result was obtained under this heading. VII.—Meats. One sample of Sausages was analysed and found genuine. In 1905 also, no percentage of impurity was recorded in this division. In addition to the above samples, officially bought and divided at the time of purchase under the Sale of Food and Drugs Acts, 14 samples of unopened Tinned and Preserved Foods were examined, and the results, which were satisfactory, were reported both to the Medical Officer of Health and to the Local Government Board. In conclusion, I may add that the Food and Drug Supply of the Borough has slightly retrograded during the year, the percentage of impurity having risen from 7.62 in 1905 to 9.28 in 1906. JOHN MUTER, PH.D., F.I.C., F.R.S.E., Public Analyst. South London Central Public Laboratory, 325, Kennington Road, S.E., January, 1907.
